		<date>2010-03-03</date>
		<username>johnnyboyz</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Not interesting and not smart; The Coen's go for heist genre frills before cheaply rounding off the character clash of classical vs. contemporary.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The first Ladykillers, a black as you like British comedy from the 1950s, was a smart; often funny and interesting film detailing a perilous situation a handful of thieves wedge their way into following a successful cash heist. It looked at generations, both past and present, and at a Post-war; post-rationing Britain as classical codes and morals of old clashed with a youth-driven Post-war 'panic' as a nation attempted to pick itself back up again following World War Two. This was epitomised by a shadowy figure stalking an elderly lady on her daily rounds, a heist mastermind, thus forcing the sky to cloud over; rain to fall and pastel drawings of Sir Winston Churchill that a busker had drawn to run as the figure of whom represents desperate, mostly young males as a collective criminal whole loomed.It isn't much surprise then, that in updating the tale from 1950s London to 2004-circa Mississippi, plus providing a range of wacky; interracial men as the thieves, that you're going to loose a lot of that substance the British original ran on. I can remember watching the original quite recently, and in knowing the Coen Brothers had remade it several years ago, it dawned on me what it was that attracted them to this sort of material: a film whose premise revolves around an immoral swiping of some money; deathly black comedic overtones; an array of oddball characters and that sense of both asking and exploring where one's morals truly lie. It's unfortunate that the 2004 remake comes off as the dud that it does, with the above characteristics resembling their subsequent film in 2008's No Country for Old Men.The set up is the same: a shifty, talkative man comes into contact with an elderly woman living a relatively lonely life in a house with which the man will covertly use as his base of operations. The gentleman on this occasion is a certain Goldthwaite Higginson Dorr (Hanks), a professor and someone we must believe has decided to attune his rather in-depth knowledge to a life of crime, although credit to The Coens for not casting a British actor in the role as the intellect-come-villain. The elderly woman, a widower and home owner is a certain Marva Munson (Hall); the film opening on her in a police station, channelling the first, in her furious telling of the police officers about how much she dislikes a lot of the contemporary music (labelling it 'Hippity-Hop'). This lack of being able to identify with what's considered modern by the current crop of youngsters in this contemporary generation sees her cite the trivialisation of racial terms acting as song lyrics.Due to the film's updating and moving away from the source text's location and era, The Ladykillers is essentially rendered a by-the-numbers heist film complete with a patched on finale that ought to be about some pretty disturbing material, and whether these characters we've just spent the majority of the film with are able to carry out such an act, but just ends up playing out in third rate slapstick fashion. It begs the question: Why bother? Why bother remaking and updating something if you're going to sap out all of what made the source text interesting and substantial? The ragtag bunch of crooks that all gather at Munson's house with Dorr arrive in an extremely eclectic nature, with one being a silent, chain smoking man from East Asia named The General (Ma); one's an African-American cleaner at the casino they're going to rob; one's an American Football player with a low IQ and one comes across as quite an upstanding, middle aged man who's an expert in demolitions. The sheer diversity of the characters, and the fact you don't buy into the fact most of them have turned to a life of crime, render most of their reason for existing in the film to act as mere sight gags: "Look, he's a simpleton" in reference to Football player Lump Hudson (Hurst); "Look, she's central European" in reference to Mountain Girl (Delano), the demolition expert's girlfriend as well as The General's constant flicking of his cigarette into his mouth.These people have nothing in common, Lump of whom need not even be there. The setting and place have been extracted, leaving anyone to fill the void and for the film to run purely on the eerie premise of whether one could kill an elderly woman and live with it if it meant inheriting a large amount of money. There's a point when the Coen's realise they've hit a dead end; when they realise sucking out everything that made the original what it is was a bad idea, and that's in the laborious nature of their playing out of everything revolving around the planning and executing of the heist, in doing so bulking up the run time and consequently excusing them from staying too long with the latter part of the film. The true crux of the original was born out of when the thieves clashed with the landlady, their reactions and behaviour to one another and the predicament when it becomes obvious the landlady knows who the gentlemen are and that they must rid the world of her due to this knowledge was the true test. Observing some of them attempt to flee, others' reactions to the finding out of this and the expressions on their faces when they realise where their choice to enter a life of crime has got them was slick and blackly funny. Here, everything is sidetracked with the only example of youth coming up against classical traditions a moment when Munson slaps Wayans' 'inside man' for coming across as the kind into 'Hippity-Hop'. Rather ironically, The Ladykillers remake itself comes across as a slap in the face for those expecting something a little smarter and a little better then what we're given.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>ShootingShark</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Bungled Casino Heist Comedy Is Uncharacteristic Coen Brothers Dud</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Marva Mudson is a god-fearing elderly widow in small-town Mississippi who rents a room to the decidedly odd Professor Dorr and his motley collection of music historians. Little does she know that these are desperate men planning a sensational crime, and are using her house as a base of operations ...There's a clichéd perspective that European comedies are subtle and mannered yet biting and satirical, whilst American comedies are brash, tasteless and moronic. If anyone could change this view it would be the ultra-talented Coen Brothers remaking a classic Ealing Studios film, but sadly they fully live up to Hollywood expectations. Where Alexander Mackendrick's 1955 original is a bright, witty, tightly-scripted and gleefully funny black comedy, this is a weak, all-over-the-place, foul-mouthed, blunderingly obvious movie. The story and character-types are pretty faithful; the mail-train robbery is now a floating casino robbery, the old lady's parrot becomes a cat and there's an unnecessarily lengthy exposition of the gang tunnelling their way to the vault, but the basic plot is sound, right down to Tom Hanks' variation on Alec Guinness' teeth. So why do I find it so poor - it's the writing I'm afraid. The Coens can write fantastic dialogue (see any scene in Miller's Crossing) and indeed some of The Professor's verbal badinage here is very amusing, but they have a weakness for making all their characters too extreme, at the expense of the inter-relationships between them and the overall tone of the film. The funniest thing in the original film is the contrast between the gang and the old dame; here all the characters are in a world of their own and they just make little speeches, alternated with pointless arguing. Each one is interesting in his or her own right but they don't gel together. The old lady seems formidable right from the start (as opposed to a helpless little caged bird) and we are never really fearful for her, so the bite of the comedy is gone. The Coens are in love with the quirkiness of their performers - Hanks' nasal snorting, Hall's bow-legged walk, Simmons' bowel problems - all of which is screwy but doesn't amount to much. They put in five minutes of gospel choir footage for no reason other than they like it - it's not got much relevance to the plot. The cast all try hard, and are fine, especially Hall in a great star turn, although the actors who come off best for me are curiously Ma and Hurst, purely because their performances consist almost entirely of blank-faced stares with hardly any dialogue. This is a lazy movie by filmmakers who could do much better - it's goofy fun, and I'd give it more points if it were by someone else, or not a remake of a vastly superior film, but the Coens' real talents are in clever, deft writing and startling yet classical visuals, and neither are on show here. Trivia - this is the Coens' eleventh movie and the first where they give themselves co-director credit (previously Joel has been listed as director and Ethan as producer). A big disappointment.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A delectably broad farce</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Not so much a remake as a radical reinterpretation of Alexander MacKendrick's original. Indeed, this displays all of the strengths and few of the weaknesses of the Coen Brothers and while not quite in the same class as "O Brother, Where Art Thou" it still stands as a fairly worthy companion piece with its Deep South setting, (as Gothic and mythical as ever), its bunch of incompetent crooks and its marvellous score.The Alec Guinness role is now in the extremely competent hands of Tom Hanks, relishing the opportunity to put on false whiskers and dentures and play as broadly as all-get-out, while the Katie Johnson character is now a Black Mama who would strike the fear of God into any self-respecting criminal and is played to the hilt by the marvellous Irma P Hall.The jokes this time round tend towards the lavatorial and the expletives are certainly not deleted but its a delectable broad farce, gorgeously photographed as ever by Roger Deakins. The difference in approach makes comparing it to the original somewhat odious; best just to savor it for what it is.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Stylistically convincing in the same vein as the Coens' "O Brother, Where Art Thou," and another seeming paean to the South, "The Ladykillers" has its moments, but it has little comic heat for such a variety of comic potential.I didn't find myself laughing much for the first two-thirds of the movie then basically found the last 20 minutes more or less (almost) worth the wait.The reason--it just lacked a consistent comic punch, even in the quirky way the Coens tend to write comedy. Yes, Tom Hanks is excellent as the loquacious and encyclopedically brilliant Professor G.H. Dorr, yet even he I did not find particularly funny. He doesn't play the Professor over-the-top exactly, but doesn't pull you in all the way either. Half the time he seemed to be mumbling his lines, if only because there were so many of them of such wild verbal density.The septet of criminal morons could have been a formula for great hilarity and I'll accept that the Coens are not the types to go for an easy laugh, but the style and tone of the movie rarely hints at humor, unlike "Raising Arizona" or the macabre humor of "Fargo." I'd forward my complaints about the movie's swearing, though surely they'd fall on deaf ears (so to speak). Some people simply are not concerned about such things, but I think it just distracts and detracts from the enjoyment of the movie. 99% of the swearing comes from Wayans and his black counterpart early in the movie. This is vaguely, if not intentionally, stereotypical.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Hanks Goes Goofy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I guess Tom Hanks wasn't satisfied with playing the (so-called) villain in 'Road To Perdition' and wanted to wear the bad guy's hat again. Or maybe he just wanted to work with the Coens badly enough that he was okay with dressing like Colonel Sanders. Hey, perhaps the one-time comic actor felt it was time to get back to his goofy root...and who writes better goofy leading characters than the Coens? Whatever the reason, Hanks plays Professor Goldthwait Higginson Dorr in 'The Ladykillers' and seems to have a hoot rattling off the patented tongue twisters in the Joel/Ethan screenplay. Hanks? The model of normalcy in a movie where he plays a thieving buffoon? Sounds like comic gold. And some of it IS quite funny, especially the dialogue and a few hilarious surprises. On the other hand, I'm surprised to report that this all-star line-up of filmmakers and star doesn't deliver an especially good film.I haven't seen the original '50s British flick with Alec Guinness and Peter Sellers, but the '04 remake is apparently true to its source. Hanks leads a rabble of dullards into the basement of a kindly southern lady's basement to systematically tunnel through the wall and into a riverboat casino office. They're after nothin' but loot, yet the little old lady thinks she's renting out her cellar to five musicians. The kicker comes when they discover they might have to follow the advice of the movie's title and off their hostess, lest she turn them in. Marlon Wayans, J.K. Simmons, Ryan Hurst, and Tzi Ma play the other crooks while Irma P. Hall steals the show as Missus Munson.Even though there are plenty of laughs in this film, the Coens can't sustain the pitch and they end up hitting a few too many snags. There's very little character development and a few too many repetitive jokes. Hanks is definitely not playing his usual upstanding hero character, but he's not threatening enough or funny enough to truly play this scruple-free faux gentleman to the hilt. Wayans is just an annoying stereotype, as is Hurst. Simmons and Hall impressed me the most, but they're pretty stupid characters too. Maybe the Coens just enjoy shaping their movies around simpletons. Their approach usually works, but 'The Ladykillers' is one of their only movies I wouldn't recommend. The last 10 or 15 minutes is unexpected and funny, even if they show us the same body-dumping sight gag once or twice too often. The delicious finale nearly turned my disappointment to joy. Nearly.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>About six months ago, I heard about a film coming out starring two-time Oscar winner Tom Hanks, Stephen Root (news radio) and J.K. Simmons (fantastic as the racist villain of HBO's Oz). This film was to be a remake of a great 50's dark comedy starring Alec Guinness and was to be directed by my favorite of the brothers-making-movies teams, the Coen brothers. Joel and Ethan Coen have directed a bunch of great movies, including Fargo and The Big Lebowski, and also one of my all-time favorites, O Brother, Where art thou? This film was to come out in April, 2004 and reestablish Tom Hanks as an exciting comic actor. The film I am talking about is of course The Ladykillers. I've anxiously awaited this movie for months, especially after seeing the hilarious trailers. I was even excited despite the presence of a Wayans brother, which generally signifies the death of any movie.Well we saw it last night. At long last I got to see what was assumed to be the Coen's next great film. After stumbling with the sell-out Intolerable Cruelty, they were gonna rise from the ashes like the proverbial phoenix and reestablish their credibility with movie geeks. After about two thirds of the movie, I had my suspicions; by the end, they were confirmed. This was definitely the worst movie the Coens (and Tom Hanks, for that matter) have ever made. Intolerable Cruelty was Oscar quality compared to The Ladykillers. At long last, after what was probably the longest streak of great films by any actor since Brando, Tom Hanks has (gasp) made a bad movie. A really bad one. I literally found nothing redeeming about this film except the gospel-filled soundtrack, which was along the same lines as O Brother.The plot, revolving around a riverboat casino heist, comes of as remarkably stupid and unbelievable. Tom Hanks puts on his best (or worst) southern accent to play some sort of cross between the devil and an annoyingly turrets-inflicted Colonel Sanders. Master thespian Marlon Wayans gangstas it up (again) as the `inside man' who somehow knows how to flawlessly patch up a huge hole in a vault wall so the casino owners can never know how the money disappeared. And then there's J.K. Simmons, who has the unlucky duty of playing a demolitions expert whose only job is to pick fight after fight with Wayons and nearly crap his pants multiple times. It's a sad thing to say that the one moment of dramatic tension in the heist scene comes when Simmons, in a rush to avoid both a tunnel-collapsing bomb and a craptacular experience in his shorts, shoots out of the tunnel and into the table containing neat stacks of all the stolen loot, which of course launches all 1.6 million dollars flying into the air at the exact same instant that the old lady at whose house the heist takes place comes through the door.The ending, which I won't spoil for those of you stupid enough to ignore my advice, is lame bordering on totally ludicrous, involving a seemingly endless, and conveniently timed, train of barges filled with garbage.It's always disheartening when a movie that was one of the most anticipated movies of the year, turns out to be this bad. I guess it just goes to show that you can have talented writers, directors, and actors, and can still end up with a movie that reeks more than the aforementioned floating garbage. * out of ****</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Ladykillers: 6/10</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>O Coen Brothers, Where Art Thou? You leave your partially indie roots to make commercial movies like The Ladykillers and Intolerable Cruelty. Although I don't consider myself to be a huge Coen Bros. fan (the oldest movie of theirs that I have seen is Fargo), I enjoy their movies, and often find their brand of dark humor very funny. Dorr, a professor who is like a cross between Col. Sanders and Foghorn Leghorn. He rooms in the house of Marva Munson (Irma P. Hall), supposedly just to room while he's studying, and use her basement to practice Renaissance music with his band. However, the real reason why he needs to be in that house is because it's adjoined to a casino that Dorr and his posse are planning to rob. Munson isn't exactly a milquetoast person, and her strict views damper the successfulness of their mission.Throughout the first hour and fifteen minutes or so, The Ladykillers seemed to just be generic and bland. A few laughs here and there, but nothing spectacular. It drew on stereotypes of the south and of race, but wasn't really anything amazing. But then the final part of the movie came, which were some of the funniest moments I've witnessed in a movie theater for a long time. THAT'S where it started getting good. The darkness in the Coens's humor came out, and put a whole new perspective on the movie. However, to get there, we needed to go through over an hour of generic movie. Some of that part even seemed like a teen comedy (I'll just say loose bowels and leave it at that).Although commended for his over-the-top performance, Hanks's character just seemed to bug me. Not only was he hard to understand (actually, almost everyone in the movie was hard to understand), but the way he laughed didn't go with the character, and I don't really think he was right for the role. Hall, however, was good in her over-the-top role. All of the crew involved with the robbery did what they needed to do, but Marlon Wayans's character seemed to belong in a completely different movie. Although occasionally funny, his typical "angry black guy" role just didn't fit in.Some scenes seemed dragged out, especially in the church, and some meaningless scenes went on for fifteen minutes without even attempting to make us laugh. And that's the point of a comedy, isn't it? The Coen Brothers know how to make comedy. They just need to do it all the way through.My rating: 6/10Rated R for language including sexual references.</fullReview>

Unless you enjoy hearing the "f" word constantly and hearing God defamed, you will need to skip this movie. I only wish I had checked it out more thoroughly before I went.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>the Coens are back on form (nearly)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I've sensed an anti-Coen brothers backlash building up recently. Most critics agree that they hit their peak with "Fargo" - though "The Big Lebowski" has a huge cult following - but their recent work has not garnered much praise. Indeed, "Intolerably Cruelty," though occasionally amusing, kinda sucked.And now here comes "The Ladykillers," already getting whacked by a number of critics who have grown impatient with the Coens' smarmy humor and lack of discipline (e.g. Ebert). But this movie does not deserve to get hit with the Coen backlash - oh no. While "Ladykillers" doesn't reach the heights of the brothers' work in the 1990s, it is, in fact, pretty damn hilarious. This time around, the critics are all wet.Sure, there's much to criticize here. A lot of the humor relies on stereotypes, which may make some people uncomfortable; personally, I detected enough irony that I was able to laugh without guilt. Let's face it, the Coens take aim at everybody here; they lampoon academics, church groups, bureaucrats, and even the foul-mouthed Wayans brother who annoys so many reviewers. Nothing is sacred here. As for the plot, it is indeed unevenly paced and lacking in traditional tension, but you know what? I'm not watching this movie for the plot; I'm delighting in the over-the-top dialogue and performances. If I want plot, I'll go watch a British movie (maybe the original "Ladykillers?").Enough of the semi-negatives. I saw brilliance in the way this movie juxtaposed opposite characters: an atheist academic with a pious Christian, an angry young man with a pompous middle-aged guy, etc. The film is full of great double acts. Granted, certain elements seem thrown in without having any particular relevance, but hey, that's just the chaos of life..."Chaos of life?" Time for me to end this review! All I can say is, I got the joke.</fullReview>

It is particularly effective during the robbery sequence itself. I might like to see this again to see how it holds up on a second viewing but, for now, I am coming down on the side of positive interpretations of the questions above. 		<date>2005-08-09</date>
		<username>gottogorunning</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>good fun comedy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Much like Intolerable Cruelty before it, the Coens' latest film relies on presupposition: inasmuch as the former capitalized on its audience's cynicism about marriage to communicate its satire, this remake of Alexander Mackendrick's 1955 comedy/crime caper assumes of its audience a working knowledge of genre tropes-particularly with noir-to orient themselves temporally before it zooms off into left field with an oblique meditation on spirituality/small-town entrapment/genre films that is as methodical and dark as anything the Coens have done.On first brush, the story is deceptively slight: an elderly church-going widow living in a mansion presided over by a portrait of the erstwhile patriarch is visited by an ingratiating figure looking for a room to let (Tom Hanks, who seems like he's shooting for the down-home erudition of Mark Twain and the preternatural look and appeal of Count Dracula). Like in Stanley Kubrick's The Killing, we are introduced to his would-be cronies, in a series of amusing situations that provides a glimpse of each character's personality and quirks. They begin drilling a hole in the cellar wall that will tunnel them directly into the riverboat's safe; along the inevitable way, they have some close-calls with the matriarch. They blow through the safe wall, the old woman discovers their plan, so they must kill her.Predictably, greed and fear prove divisive and the crooks begin dropping like flies-but decidedly unpredictably, the little old lady seems enveloped by a godly force that thwarts their best-laid and seemingly ineluctable plans. Example: A killer looms with piano wire over her sleeping figure. Mere inches from her neck, a Jesus figurine leaps from a clock, causing him to swallow his cigarette, choke on her dentures in a beside water glass and fall headlong down the stairs when Pickles the cat darts before his legs. I'm positive many critics will jump all over this scenario and others subsequently like it by citing it as part and parcel of the filmmakers' obsession with murderdeathkill (I wouldn't argue that they often highlight scenes of death as punch-lines illustrating the futility of their characters, a peculiar trait of auto-assassination and self-critique that seems enlisted for entertainment's sake)--and it is that, but to think the film's implications can and should be swallowed there is a mistake; the situation depends entirely too much on serendipity.As the bodies pile up, they are dropped from a bridge into a passing repository ship that moves languidly between the town and a trash-heap situated, ironically, on an idyllic island. The Coens' and cinematographer Roger Deakins are deliberately obscure in their depiction of the town--the downtown is a series of ramshackle shops with no signs of life, the police station is framed in medium close-up, a rectangle of bricks seemingly isolated, and the church sits atop a hill, an unspectacular white building--and signify it in a handful of archetypal shots, which signify entropy, a town without an identity, without spatial or temporal referent.The criminals die in increasingly unusual and unbelievable ways--Hanks, for his part, dies in a romantic triumph, concurrently the sole living proprietor of the stolen booty and reciting to himself a line of Poe. Looking up, he sees a raven situated atop a gargoyle, and we see the foundation is crumbling. As he continues the recitation, the statue falls and knocks him headlong into the boat, cruising to the rhythm and exact timing of his death; trash in, trash out. Crime doesn't pay, right? By following this time-honored platitude in as subversive and postmodern a way as possible, the Coens' deus ex machina is one completely informed by and keeping in strict compliance with genre code--they may have cheated to get there, but the Coens have made a true anomaly: an intellectually lazy and meta-textually provocative portraiture of life as a poor, confined genre piece. The inanimate genre as empathic character.</fullReview>
	</review>

I just saw Ladykillers, and frankly I'm slightly taken back by the bad reviews it got. Now, the way I see it, the film falls in a Grey area - it's far too commercial and mainstream for the Coens' fans, and too much of an oddball movie for those looking for a mainstream comedy. Certainly, this isn't the Coens at their best - there's no use comparing Ladykillers to masterpieces like Barton Fink, Big Lebowski, Fargo, Miller's Crossing, Hudsucker Proxy and O Brother, Where Art Thou? or even to lesser Coen projects like The Man Who Wasn't There, Intolerable Cruelty and Raising Arizona. In hindsight, Ladykillers probably is the duo's weakest offering to date. But even when the Coens are at their very worst, they're still the best around. Even when dealing with a weak storyline and one-dimensional characters - and for the first time in their career, the story isn't one of their own - their brilliant, one-of-a-kind scriptwriting and directing make up for it. Nobody writes - or directs - a dialogue like Joel and Ethan. And even though they weren't able to arouse sympathy for any of the characters - not even the charming old woman - they still created some unique, memorable, hilarious characters in the best Coen tradition. Tzi Ma (The Quiet American), Marlon Wayans (Requiem For A Dream), Ryan Hurst (Remember The Titans) and J. K. Simmons (Spider-Man) are each uniquely terrific as the band of robbers, and Irma P. Hall is wonderful and hilarious as a classic god-fearing Big Mama. The show-stopper is Mr. Tom Hanks who, as Professor G. H. Dorr, grants one of his most memorable performances. Tom Hanks' characters usually have a Tom Hanks attitude about them; for this role his disconnected from that, and truly I haven't enjoyed him this much since Forrest Gump. The Coens also do as gorgeous a job with recurring motives as they usually do - in this case, a cat, a hideous statue, and a portrait that seems to change a bit whenever you look at it. The surreal, oddball atmosphere of a Coen Brothers classic is still there - more so, I would say, than in Intolerable Cruelty and even The Man Who Wasn't There. The feeling and the style are there; unfortunately, as I mentioned earlier, the film suffers from a weak plot, which only become hilariously funny in the last half hour.So you're more than welcome to follow the dozens of one and two star reviews on this page, if you like; I still think Ladykillers is well worth watching for Coen fans - AS LONG AS YOU REMAIN OBJECTIVE AND IGNORE THE COEN NAME ON IT! (Yeah, that's a tricky one, I know.) If you like the Coens' oddball humor and surrealism, just keep that 'oh, whatever happened to them?' thought out of your head and enjoy the movie. And I'm quite sure you'll enjoy it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Mybookshelf</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Let's Just Pretend Allen Smithee Directed It</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I do not recommend this movie to comedy fans and have, instead, included recommendations of better Coen Brothers films below. However...I do recommend The Ladykillers to aspiring filmmakers as a valuable study. This film is an excellent example of what happens when the director has problems getting all the actors (and production crew) to share the same vision for the TONE of the movie. In that respect, it is similar to "She Devil" where all of the actors had wildly different interpretations of the overall comedic tone. Both films illustrate how important it is for the director to NOT ONLY have a distinct vision but how essential it is to select a cast that share and support the director's interpretation of the material. In Hollywood film director's frequently replace their name with the moniker "Allen Smithee" on films they wish to be disassociated from. The Coen Brothers didn't in this case so you have to admire them for standing by it. Although this turkey does alter their otherwise near perfect run of creating some of the most distinctly unique films of their generation they still have a remarkable batting average. I would hate for this film to be anyone's introduction to the Coen Brother's otherwise brilliant body of work, so skip The Ladykillers unless you are viewing it as a lesson in what went wrong. If you are interested in exploring Coen Brothers comedies I suggest:"Oh Brother Where Art Thou" "Intollerable Cruelty" "Raising Arizona" "Barton Fink" ...which are all visually unique as well as side-splitting funny, and brilliantly executed. They all hit home runs with their pitch-perfect tone. I also loved Hudsucker Proxy but it isn't for everyone. Fans of 1930's screwball comedies are likely to appreciate it though. Jennifer Jason Leigh performance is an homage to screwball legend Kate Hepburn and the famous fast-talking literary wit Dorothy Parker. "The Big Lebowski" is especially appreciated by people familiar with Southern California culture but also a cult favorite with laid back dudes and underachievers everywhere. The bowling lane scenes with John Turturro are so visually quirky and hilarious you will laugh until your ribs hurt. Their excellent dramas include their debut "Blood Simple" and "Miller's Crossing." Last of all, if you have been trapped under a rock and somehow missed "Fargo" run as fast as you can and buy or rent it right now! It is the Coen Brother's creme-de-la-creme of film-making achievement.</fullReview>

		<date>2005-03-02</date>
		<username>TheNorthernMonkee</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>Tedious Hollywood Remake - Avoid</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>SPOILERS Ealing Studios produced in their height, some of British cinema's truly great comedies. Often staring Alec Guiness, Stanley Holloway or Alastair Sim, these films were comedy classics in every sense of the word. So, it shouldn't really be a surprise that in a modern Hollywood where so many films are sequels or remakes, that tedious and irritating remakes of classics are going to happen. In 2004, relocating the story from England to the American South, the normally decent Coen Brothers, have created an abominable remake of Ealing classic "The Ladykillers".Marva Munson (Irma P Hall) is your typical southern old lady. Widowed and spending most of her time at Church, Mrs Munson is a kind old lady who simply wants what's best for everybody. One day, the intellectual Professor G.H.Dorr (Tom Hanks) enters her life with a view to renting a room. Now as Mrs Munson continues her everyday life, the Professor and his partners in crime plot to steal a fortune from a casino. As you'd expect though, not everything runs to plan.It's often remarked that remakes are always inferior to the original. Whilst there are definite exceptions to the rule, the Coen Brothers' remake of "The Ladykillers" doesn't help the case for remakes. Relocated to a religious deep south, from the instant the music kicks in, the signs are there that this film is going to be annoying.From the introduction of the little old lady, the film continues to progress until the introduction of Hanks' character. Taking over from Alec Guiness, Tom Hanks, one of the finest actors of his generation, annoys from the instant that his character appears. Removing all of the tension that Guiness provided, this NEW professor is an Edgar Allan Poe fan who is so well educated, that often the characters don't have a clue what he says, and we don't care. Worse of all is the characters laugh. Sounding like an asthmatic schoolboy, Hanks manages to somehow make an already annoying character, even more infuriating. To his credit, Hanks has gone on record as saying he never watched the original before this remake, purely so that he wasn't biast. A noble thought and an idea which some stars of remakes should take on board. In this case however, perhaps Hanks might have been better studying the exemplary performance by Guiness. He might have learnt a thing or two.Another major flaw with this film is just how infuriating the supporting performances by Marlon Wayans and J.K. Simmons are. Wayans spends his entire screen time acting like he's "cool" and forever swearing. There's nothing wrong with a film containing swearing when it's appropriate (comedies like "South Park" and more serious films like "Reservoir Dogs"), but throughout this film Wayans is at it non-stop and often it's completely irrelevant and pointless. J.K. Simmons as well also annoys from his first appearance. Originally giving the impression of being the remake equivalent of Major Courtney from the original, Simmons plays a character who spends large amounts of his time suffering from Irritable Bowel Syndrome. Whatever the feelings of a person towards this condition, the way this film constantly has Simmons needing the toilet is neither funny or relevant. It is yet another pointless addition to a script designed to force laughter out of a public who know better.On a positive note, it is true that the way the film introduces the characters by giving them their own little back history is cleverly done, and Tzi Ma as The General is funny throughout. Ma's trick of hiding the cigarette in his mouth is definitely overused, but there are reasons behind it's consistency, and by constantly keeping a stern face and sporting an Adolf Hitler moustache, he does stand out as being one of the few bright points in this woeful film.If a remake is done well, so remarkably rare that you'd think Hollywood would learn it's lesson by now, then it can be a rewarding encounter. Sadly though, this Americanised remake of a British classic is not just bad, but hideous. Led by a surprisingly dire Tom Hanks, it grates on the nerves throughout, and by the end you wonder whether the Coen Brothers should ever be allowed to work in Hollywood again. This is a film which should be avoided at all costs.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A Decent Time Killer</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Whatever you think of this movie you have to admit it has the feel of a Coen brothers movie in the way that it unites odd characters with the south, all having their own quirks and charms. I have not seen the original - though I plan to since this is supposedly vastly different.MINOR SPOILERSThe problem was that the Coens' do not know how to portray black people well, and it shows indeed in this film (and explains why they rarely appear in their other films) with Irma P. Hall's character and particularly Marlon Wayans' character. I do not blame the acting, in fact everyone did fine for what their roles were, but Wayans' character swore a blue streak but it was stereotypical black male speech and wasn't funny (and seemed particularly out of place with the rest of the film, as was the case with the commercial director scene as well) whereas Irma P. Hall's character was a "mammy" character who is gullible and blindly religious. Other blacks in the film were also stereotyped - small time criminals, abusive parents and zealous church folk, both different extremes, but extremes nonetheless. Also, I wish that the music from the gospel choir factored into the movie more than it did - it seemed like an excuse to put music in but didn't seem organic as the Coens have done in their other films (i.e. O Brother Where Art Thou?). I also was beginning to really feel sympathy for Wayans' character when his childhood is explained but then all is lost as Hall's character slaps him a few more times - which seemed like it would have provoked Wayans to shoot her rather than associate her with his mother, whom he seems to fear as a child yet love (?) as an adult.That's not to say this movie wasn't funny, so long as you like dark comedy (including but not limited to: dogs dying, thumbs getting blown off, several murders, accidental deaths and suicides and several injuries and childhood trauma). It did seem like it was trying really hard to be funny at certain parts, but there were some also really well filmed scenes - in particular I really enjoyed the introduction of Lump's character since it is all POV and reminded me of Bill Plympton's animation style, only in live action...too bad that Lump's character was otherwise needless and undeveloped. What makes this film great, aside from the performances (Hanks was particularly interesting to watch since he was not only a villain but also over the top) is the spectacular cinematography which really sells the feel of the romanticized modern south, which the Coens are well known for.All in all this is a decent film, not great, not bad. Worth a watch for Coen brothers and/or dark comedy fans. But you only need to see it once.OVERALL RATING: 6 out of 10 stars.</fullReview>

The Coen Bros seem to be on a low streak of late. Neither this nor Intolerable Cruelty were bad films but they were more films of good scenes and performances than a complete movie. 		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>The Grace of the Deus ex Machina</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Much like Intolerable Cruelty before it, the Coens' latest film relies on presupposition: inasmuch as the former capitalized on its audience's cynicism about marriage to communicate its satire, this remake of Alexander Mackendrick's 1955 comedy/crime caper assumes of its audience a working knowledge of genre tropes?particularly with noir?before it zooms off into left field with an oblique meditation on spirituality/small-town entrapment/genre films that is as methodical and dark as anything the Coens have done.At first glance, the story is deceptively slight: an elderly church-going widow living in a mansion presided over by a portrait of the erstwhile patriarch is visited by an ingratiating figure looking for a room to let (Tom Hanks, who seems like he's shooting for the down-home erudition of Mark Twain and the preternatural look and appeal of Count Dracula). As in Kubrick's The Killing, we are introduced to his would-be cronies through a series of amusing situations that provides a glimpse of each character's personality and quirks. They begin drilling a hole in the cellar wall that will tunnel them directly into the riverboat's safe; along the inevitable way, they have some close calls with the matriarch. They blow through the safe wall, the old woman discovers their plan, and so they must kill her.Predictably, greed and fear prove divisive and the crooks begin dropping like flies?but decidedly unpredictably, the little old lady seems enveloped by a godly force that thwarts their best-laid and seemingly ineluctable plans. Example: a killer looms with piano wire over her sleeping figure. Mere inches from her neck, a Jesus figurine leaps from a clock, causing him to swallow his cigarette, choke on her dentures in a beside water glass and fall headlong down the stairs when Pickles the cat darts before his legs. I'm positive many critics will jump all over this scenario and others subsequently like it by citing it as part and parcel of the filmmakers' obsession with murder-death-kill (I wouldn't argue that they often highlight scenes of death as punch-lines illustrating the futility of their characters, a peculiar trait of auto-assassination and self-critique that seems enlisted for entertainment's sake)?and it is that, but to think the film's implications can and should be swallowed there is a mistake; the situation depends entirely too much on serendipity.As the bodies pile up, they are dropped from a bridge into a passing repository ship that moves languidly between the town and a trash heap situated, ironically, on an idyllic island. The Coens and cinematographer Roger Deakins are deliberately obscure in their depiction of the town?the downtown is a series of ramshackle shops with no signs of life, the police station is framed in medium shots, a rectangle of bricks seemingly isolated, and the church sits atop a hill, an unspectacular white building?showing it in a handful of archetypal shots, which collectively signify entropy, a town without an identity, without spatial or temporal referent.The criminals die in increasingly unusual and unbelievable ways. Hanks, for his part, dies in romantic triumph, concurrently the sole living proprietor of the stolen booty and reciting to himself a line of Poe. Looking up, he sees a raven situated atop a gargoyle, and we see the foundation is crumbling. As he continues the recitation, the statue falls and knocks him headlong into the boat, cruising to the rhythm and exact timing of his death; trash in, trash out. Crime doesn't pay, right? By following this time-honored platitude in as subversive and postmodern a way as possible, the Coens' deus ex machina is one completely informed by and keeping in strict compliance with genre code. They may have cheated to get there, but the Coens have made a true anomaly: a convention-compliant, meta-textually conversant portrait of life as a poor, confined genre piece.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Stale for the most part</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It would be impossible to dislike a film like this considering who made it and who stars in it but I kept asking myself if the comedy that was presented to me on screen was original? For the most part it's not. Story is about a thief in Mississippi named Professor Goldthwait Higginson Dorr (Tom Hanks) who rents a room from an elderly woman named Marva Munson (Irma P. Hall) but Dorr really wants to use her root cellar to tunnel into a riverboat casino and rob it. Dorr tells her that he is part of a band that plays religious music and that they will use her cellar to practice in. The rest of his cohorts are Pancake (J.K. Simmons), The General (Tzi Ma), Lump (Ryan Hurst), and Gawain (Marlon Wayans) and they all bring their expertise to the heist.*****SPOILER ALERT*****After succeeding in stealing over a million dollars and covering up the tunnel Mrs. Munson finds out who they really are so now they decide to kill her but this proves to be a much more difficult task than it appears.This film is directed by Ethan and Joel Coen and it's one of the reasons why this keeps from being a complete failure. The other reason is the casting of Hanks in a role so over the top that it does become fun to watch and he genuinely seems to be having the time of his life portraying a character that seems to have jumped out of "Gone With The Wind". Hall is a delight as the church going widow who often talks to the painting of her deceased husband and the expression on the face of the painting often changes depending on the events. Hall is a believable force and we can all agree that she would be trouble for anyone but even her character is not solidly written. The rest of the cast comes across as more cartoonish than funny and their is no subtle actions by any of them. Did the film really need Simmons character to have irritable bowel syndrome? I think we can all agree that this film is not one of the better efforts by the Coen brothers when they resort to bathroom humor. But on the other hand the film does offer their trademark dark humor and the disposing of all the bodies is an example of that. I can't argue with anyone that tells me they find this funny because the actors are all fun to watch on a very silly level but I found this to be a more stale effort considering the amount of talent involved behind it. The original 1955 film with Alec Guinness was more subtle and droll but this remake is wildly animated and over the top. This comedy is more depending on your type of humor but I do think that there are to many uninspired scenes. Also, that's Eddie Murphy in a wig as the church choir director in an uncredited cameo.</fullReview>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>What happened to the Coen Brothers' cleverness and originality?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As if the title isn't enough to get the gist of my critique . . . I am at a loss, after last year's dismal "Intolerable Cruelty" and now "The Ladykillers," as to where the Coens' storytelling and stylization prowess has gone. There are hints of their classic morbidly funny style in "The Ladykillers," but those small hints, plus constant collaborator T-Bone Burnett's musical supervision, are the only reminders that you are watching a Coen Brothers movie, or even a movie of the Coen Brothers caliber.What made "Miller's Crossing," "Fargo," "Barton Fink," "Raising Arizona," "O Brother, Where Art Thou" and "The Big Lebowski" such great films is the Coen Brothers' greatness at writing clever scripts that pay homage to and satirize many different genres in film. As strange as Coen Brothers characters are in these movies, they aren't caricatures per se. They have dreams, dilemmas and dimensions. You'd be hard-pressed to find a well-written character in "The Ladykillers." Tom Hanks is fun as an erudite Southern gentleman criminal, but you can see that he's clearly trying too hard. And he's the most defined and complete character in the film. The idea of the Coen Brothers resorting to stupid bathroom humor is appalling. Are they simply phoning in their efforts to make a bigger Hollywood paycheck? "Let's use bigger Hollywood names, get outside help in writing boring Hollywood-reject scripts, and draw bigger box office dollars while working less?" Do they have another full-time job that requires their attention?The Coen Brothers used to write their own scripts and come up with their own ideas, even when they were adapting from another medium. I simply don't know what they're doing any more, other than really slipping in their craftsmanship.</fullReview>

		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Not the Coen's best, but still good...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Joel and Ethan seem to be sliding a bit from the earlier brilliance demonstrated by such films as "Miller's Crossing" or "The Hudsucker Proxy." Their films of late have contained weaker plots and less fulfilling pay-offs which could be attributed to the fact that their last two movies have been remakes. "Intolerable Cruelty" was re-written and cleaned up by the Coens and then produced. "The Ladykillers" was an old 1955 British movie that the Coens bought the rights to and re-vamped. While all the Coen signs are there (witty dialogue, character development, dialects, etc.) the movie still lacks in the area of the final pay-off.Each of the final incidents of the film get farther and farther from reality. The film opens on a realistic note and closes with a strangely pieced together "deux ex machina." The stereotypical Coen darkness is still in tact, but it comes at a much heavier price considering the fact that is breaks the otherwise believable mold of the film.Tom Hanks is amazing in the film. His accent and acting have never been better, and he should be nominated for an Oscar for the work he obviously put into his character. The music is wonderful, reminiscent of the "O Brother Where Art Thou?" soundtrack while being completely contemporary. None of the script suffers, as the witty banter between the characters is never lost on the audience. The Coens also do a wonderful job of mixing the high and low comedy, using word puns and double entendre from Tom Hanks and physical comedy from Marlon Wayons and J.K. Simmons.Overall, the film is enjoyable to watch and is better than your average run-of-the-mill Hollywood comedy. But for the Coens', it is disappointing. As a big fan, I expect to see the same greatness that made "Fargo" a sleeper hit and "Miller's Crossing" a cult classic. Unfortunately, the Coens have not been living up to their earlier brilliance, which may be difficult since their earlier films were nearly flawless. I give "The Ladykillers" a 7 out of 10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>The Perfect Score: Coen Style</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>*** The Perfect Score: Coen Style ***POSSIBLE SPOILERSI went to see the teen movie The Perfect Score about a month before seeing this movie, and to be quite honest; there wasn't much of a difference.Both movies focused around a group that are unknown to each other and come together to pull a difficult heist, one for SAT answers, the other for adult themes, money. Both have one Asian, one Black guy and one-dimensional characters that give us nothing more than a bland comedy that vainly tries to be more than it is. The only notable difference is that there are not any girls in the Ladykiller's gang (unless you count Mountain Girl) and that the IMDB uses will give this better rating because the name Coen is attached to it.It seems, however, that our good indie friends the Coen Brothers have found themselves in a bit of a rut. Either that or the success of O' Brother has flattened their taste a bit. Either way, Ladykillers is, at least to me, the worst movie they have put to screen yet. I say this with a great deal of despair since I own each release they have put out since their original Blood Simple.Tom Hanks is over-the-top as The Professor, a Latin reading, Edgar Allan Poe reciting, charming double-talking sociopath who rents a room from a church going black Mississippi lady who has a basement that just happens to be the ideal place to stage a casino robbery. Normally I enjoy over-the-top, hammy villain performances such as Al Pachino's Big Boy Caprice in Dick Tracy and Anthony Hopkins in the two post-Silence Of The Lambs movies, Red Dragon and Hannibal. Hanks was different somehow, in that, although charming, he didn't exude the right flavor to entice my morbid curiosity towards hammy villains. In the realm of double-talkers though, George Clooney was much, much better in O' Brother.With character names like The Professor, The General, Garth Pancake, Mountain Girl and Lump, you know these guys aren't going to be the most fleshed out of characters. In fact, I would say that this movie has the worst stereotypes of any Coen Brothers movie. They've had stereotypical characters before, but have always put a unique spin on either the character or how they are used in this movie.Other problems: the casting was weak. Marlon Waynes is not up to the Coen's stage-like script format, slurs too many words, cusses too harshly and in general cannot act past a stupid hardheaded scoundrel thug-wannabe potsmoking scumbag. His best performance of the movie was after he was shot and face didn't flicker when he died; I was impressed that he could do it. J.K. Simmons as Garth Pancake was also an annoyance. His weak bowels leaked towards the silly fart humor that I didn't expect nor want to see out of a Coen Brothers film. His testimonies towards his condition were laughable in a bad way. Jon Polito would have done a better job with the role and I hope he was unavailable to do the film so I could extend some credibility. The General, played by Tzi Ma and Lump, played by Ryan Hurst are one star characters. Who knows if they could of acted better if they weren't delegated to cheap one-liners without any true explanation as to why they're there. Both are capable with the comedy timing, but both grew thin and wearisome by the end.The church scenes were useless. The gospel music will entice any fan of the said genre, however, for the sake of the movie it was nothing more than filler material. It didn't drive the plot, set the mood or a standard. The only plot device it held was that it showed Irma leaving the church early so she could set-up tea for her church friends and accidentally soil a near-perfect casino robbery. But we didn't need a five minute gospel hymn to show us that. Ten to fifteen seconds would have been sufficient. It is possible that the Coans are trying to cash in on some more potential soundtrack money after the unexpected success of the O' Brother soundtrack. Where that movie was cute and clever with it's usage of music, this is blowing towards the direction of a prettily shot documentary of the Mississippi black church community.I miss the Coen regulars. Francis and the aforementioned Jon hasn't been around since The Man Who Wasn't There, Steve Buscemi and John Goodman haven't been seen since The Big Lebowski, if I'm not mistaken and John Turturro since O' Brother. With the chemistry these people must have from working together, or at least, around each other for so long, it makes it seem that this new bunch lacks the necessary developed work relationship to make scenes work as well as they did in the past.One thing, though, is people's complaints about the language of this movie, and then compares it to The Big Lebowski. Does anyone not remember Walter's relationship with Donny? This is not the first foul-mouthed Coen Brothers movie and we'll probably get another one down the line. Who cares and why? No one called this family material.All in all, even though, unlike Cruel Intentions and The Hudsucker Proxy, which are also Coen Brothers Lite, this ultimately fails. I hope next time they decide to take a little more time in-between movies and give us something a little more worth while to watch. It's a good thing we still have the original to watch, those of us that are willing to watch something nearly fifty years old at the time of this writing.I suppose it's time to wish for the good old days of Miller's Crossing and Raising Arizona. 5/10~Joe</fullReview>

Incidentally, am I the only poster to find the heavy-handed stereotyping of all the black characters inane and offensive? Unless you are a die-hard fan of either Hanks or the Cohen brothers I urge you to give this one a miss.</fullReview>

		<username>unbend_5440</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A Coen Brothers comeback (but still a little off)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have to say that The Ladykillers is the first time I've really enjoyed a Coen Brothers movie since Fargo. I found that with their last few movies, it was mostly focused on quirky characters and unusually amusing circumstances. That is of course what the Coen's do best, but for a while now they've lost focus on stories. That was the big problem with Intolerable Cruelty. And while I do say that this is the first time I've really enjoyed one of their movies since Fargo, I still think Ladykillers came up short in a lot of ways. The script and their direction puts them back on track, yet I wonder if the Coen's will ever capture the brilliance they displayed so well in Fargo and Raising Arizona.Tom Hanks does give one of his most interesting performances ever. I would say that no other actor could have pulled off something this different, but honestly, I don't know how Tom Hanks even pulled it off. He's so eccentric and complicated. Hanks took a fantastically scripted character and injected an excessive amount of personality into it. The Coen's have a gift for writing the smartest and most original dialogue. If Hanks does get an Oscar nomination for this, and he probably should, the Coen's will be just as much responsible for it. This movie could be the one character speaking for 2 hours straight, and it would never lose my attention. Even with the most intelligent and complicated dialogue using the biggest words in the dictionary, I never lost focus on what he was saying. That's the thing I really appreciate about The Ladykillers. Even when he was spouting off speeches and I had no idea what was being said, I still was able to follow the story and appreciate the performance by Hanks. This brings me to the big complaint I have with The Ladykillers.Marlon Wayans almost ruins this movie. In fact, it's his fault that I'm even writing a complaint in this review. While I was able to follow Tom Hanks no matter how intelligent or complex his dialogue got, I spaced out whenever Marlon Wayans would be speaking. It's kind of sad that I could pay attention to Hanks when his dialogue was overly intelligent, and when Marlon Wayans would start ranting in childish profanity, I would space right out. For a movie that seems to pride itself on how mature and intelligent it is, it throws all dignity out the window when Marlon Wayans is on screen. He doesn't have one intelligent thing to say. His language is very inappropriate as well. I'm not saying that I find the words he's saying to be inappropriate. The problem is the wild contrast between the smart and witty screenplay, and the childish shock language Wayans uses. The two styles do not fit together. This movie and the Coen Brothers are above the dumb Scary Movie name calling that is way too prominent in this film. I also really doubt that the Coen's wrote Wayans dialogue. I have a terrible feeling that they cast Marlon Wayans and told him to just improvise and be funny. If that is the case, I give the Coen's more credit as writers, but I subtract a lot of credit for their competence as Directors. They must know that Wayans didn't belong in this movie.Just as much as Tom Hanks deserves an Oscar for his role, so does Irma Hall. Even when the eccentricities of the story become a little unrealistic, she always grounds it back in reality. Lots of these performances are a slightly exaggerated, perhaps even parodies, but Irma Hall is authentic and believable at all times. She sounds like she's making up a lot of her dialogue on the spot. This is a great performance. I also loved JK Simmons. But my second complaint revolves around his character. The Irritable Bowel Syndrome stuff??? That was completely unnecessary. And like Marlon Wayans, it drags the level of class that the Coen's have down to a childish gross-out level. This movie needed to be mature and funny all the way through. It lost me when it lost it's maturity. But I do have to say that one of JK Simmons best moments was when he described his Irritable Bowels Singles Groups. That was funny, but the character quirk was not. Ryan Hurst and Tzi Ma were maybe the funniest ones in the cast, and they have very little to say. Ryan Hurst is doing a bit of a takeoff on the cartoon-like dummies. He's big and strong and burly, but has no brains in his head and often blanks out. Tzi Ma will speak maybe once every 30 minutes, but when he does, I would always crack up.The climax is very creative and hilarious. Minus the Marlon Wayans flashback, which also didn't fit right. The ongoing garbage barge bit got funnier each time. It felt very refreshing to sit in a theatre and laugh at a smart adult oriented comedy, instead of the overly repetitive childish foul gross out jokes....... which........ This movie also has?????. Mostly good, though.</fullReview>

		<date>2012-05-25</date>
		<username>zardoz-13</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>The Coens Blow It!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen brothers don't hit the mark every time and their uninspired remake of the 1955 British Ealing original comedy serves as an example. This handsomely produced homage to director Alexander Mackendrick's classic is not without some shred of humor, but "The Ladykillers" doesn't show the Coens' at the top of their form. Tom Hanks is ideally cast as a smooth-talking, Southern hustler named Professor Goldthwait Higginson Dorr who is in fact a criminal mastermind. Indeed, Hanks seems to be channeling the blowhard Warner Brothers' cartoon character Foghorn Leghorn as he tangles with Irma P. Hall's God-fearing, gospel singing matriarchal. Dorr and his dubious accomplices use the cellar of widower Marva Munson after she takes him into her home as a boarder. Dorr and company pretend to be practicing music while they tunnel toward the vault of a riverboat casino. Naturally, they encounter one form of adversity after another as they indulge in their criminal enterprise. Although they fare successfully in their robbery, they are not so fortunate in sharing in the proceeds of their daring scheme. The matronly heroine outwits them at every turn and the harder that they try to dispose of her after she discovered their perfidy proves fruitless.In the words of one of the conspirators named Pancake, this remake should have been "the easiest thing in the world," but the Coens blow it. When they aren't being heavy handed, they are just plain ridiculous. The collection of criminals that Dorr assembles are a motley collection of losers. Pancake (J.K. Simmons of "Spider-Man") is a demolitions expert who blows his own finger off when he tries to blast a rock that blocks the tunnel that they are digging to the underground vault of the Bandit Queen. The man that Dorr has an insider, Gawain (Marlon Wayans), is a smut-mouthed, sex-obsessed janitor on the Bandit Queen with all the information that they require for their robbery. Along with Pancake and Gawain are two others, a thick-headed oaf named Lump (Ryan Hurst) and a tight-lipped, cigarette-puffing former North Vietnamese officer called The General ( Tzi Ma) who knows everything about tunnels owing to his experiences in Vietnam when the French were fighting the war. The cretinous fools are the worst criminals on record and the irony is that a harmless old lady outsmarts them. She winds up with $1.6 million and donates it to Bob Jones University, a religious institution of higher learning. The performances are top-notch as are the production values. The fault lies with the Coens who are tone deaf this time around. If they were hoping to reproduce the hit that they had with their previous Mississippi set comedy "O Brother Where Art Thou?," they failed miserably.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>adebimpe jonah</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>A bit of a let-down</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This movie is an update of the 1955 British comedy, but it seems lost in stylized tics and windy verbiage that never seem to expire.Tom Hanks plays a role as the Southern-gentleman mastermind of a riverboat casino heist should flow effortlessly but isn't particularly engaging--there's no sense of amusing malice in his Professor G.H. Dorr, though he's portrayed, unconvincingly, as some sort of absolute evil--and he doesn't seem to want to connect with much of the cast (though there is some decent interplay with his nemesis, Marva Munson, the landlady whose home he uses as his base of operation, played in over-sized fashion by Irma P. Hall, bucking for an Oscar). The upshot is that Hanks' obstinacy actually slows the rhythms of the film to a crawl and you find yourself increasingly impatient. As for the film itself, it's another empty academic exercise from the Coen Brothers, with elegant art direction by Richard Johnson and clever cinematography from Roger Deakins (the camera angles get skewed as the situation gets darkly violent) but also a misanthropic theological overview: though characters fall from grace (into a garbage barge), the heaven they're cast from is an empty, ghettoized deep-South parish that appears unclean and undesirable. They also possess a cluelessness as to what's funny, smarmily assuming jokes about irritable bowel syndrome are audience-pleasers; and their attempts at surrealist humor (most notably the varying expressions of a portrait meant to comment on the proceedings) falls dismally flat. With a sour Marlon Wayans, Tzi Ma (whose tricks with cigarettes contain the film's most entertaining moments), J.K. Simmons (from 'Spider-Man') and Ryan Hurst as Hanks' partners in crime; they're severely let down by the Coens' indifferent treatment.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Very good but not great Coen effort</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What the Coen brothers do best, they do uniformly well, and the dialogue, character development and caper plot all click along perfectly. Tom Hanks shows us that even when he's doing something just for fun, without much serious effort, he can still be amazing to watch. The Coens have a lot of fun themselves, reversing roles (racial and otherwise; if you're not familiar with Bob Jones University, read up on it for an extra note of irony) and showing the unifying theme about the bad kharma that curses this particular enterprise and the way God, Fate, whatever you want to believe (the movie leaves it all up to you) controls your destiny and sometimes even shows favoritism. Jjensen1's criticism was unfair, apparently that person showed up expecting more action and being unfamiliar with the Coens, was woefully impatient with the character development and the slice-of-life scenes. Look for some clever metaphorical touches, like the gospel choir (and look for some R&amp;B and gospel greats in *those* scenes), the ever-changing portrait of Marva's husband, the garbage scow headed to the dump (which the preacher also describes). Granted there is one major downfall and some minor ones. The major one: just the way the plot is constructed keeps you from caring about the characters and what happens to them, in the fascinating way the Coen Brothers usually make us do even in movies like "Fargo". There's also an issue of very foul language, granted it's important for the sake of some of the characters but still could've been toned down a bit. Overall, I would recommend this movie as a worthy Coen effort even if it's not one of their best.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>"The Ladykillers" is the latest offering from Joel and Ethan Coen. This movie is clever and well-written. Tom Hanks stars as Prof. G.H. Dorr, Ph.D., a criminal mastermind who leads his gang in a plot to rob a riverboat casino. Hanks plays this role to the hilt, with the look of an antebellum Southern gentleman, and flowery dialogue. Other colorful characters include Marlon Wayans as Gawain McSam, the casino's janitor and the gang's "inside man," and J.K. Simmons as Garth Pancake, the hapless explosives expert. Then there's Irma P. Hall as Mrs. Munson, the church-going, no-nonsense lady who's housing Dorr and his crew. 		<date>2004-03-27</date>
		<username>Llamapoot1</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Colorful moral fable with lots of big laughs: not without its flaws, but very entertaining</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As another reviewer commented, I missed quite a few lines of dialogue because everyone in the audience was laughing so loudly (myself included), and that goes to show one of the greater strengths of the Coen brothers' new film. It is really truly ridiculously funny. The humor is broad at times, often dark, often quirky and bizarre, and may not appeal to an audience that cares for more routine humor and straightfoward punchlines or pandering to the lowest common denomenator. Much of the humor comes from the Coens' trademark dialogue. As usual it is immaculately manicured, beautiful to behold, and hilarious to hear: their use of colloquialisms, slang, jargon, accents, dialects, archaic language, and just plain funny words that one may or may not have ever heard before is simply tremendous. And when put into the mouths of capable character actors those words work magic on my funny bone.The casting, again as usual, is awesome. The whole cast pulls off the not so easy task of creating consistent caricatures whose absurd actions they have to make sense of.However, my favorite part of the film was the highly stylized narrative style. It felt more like a fable or moral tale than any kind of story that one is supposed to take as seriously realistic. The film is set in Mississippi, but presents itself as more of an imagined caricature or archetype constructed of various images and anachronisms that add up to some vague notion of "Mississippiness", rather than an actual earthly location. Whatsmore, the obviously allegorical qualities of the principle characters make for some delightful religious and literary symbolism, that treads the fine line of parody: seeming at once oddly serious but with a healthy dose of dark parodic humor.All in all I recommend this movie rather highly. Mainly because it is so different from the formulaic, prepatterned, pandering drivel that constitutes the majority of mainstream comedy film. It is nice to see a screwball farce that is not afraid of being misunderstood, that does not have to follow the lead of whatever popular trash came before it, and that is willing to at least attempt to conflate broad low-brow humor with intelligently crafted structure, content, and satiric wit.9 out of 10...I hardly ever stopped laughing.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I really enjoyed this movie. I can't remember laughing so hard for so long at any movie recently. Tom Hanks is almost unrecognizable as G.H. Dorr, PhD, with teeth that cry for orthodontia and a vocabulary that screams for a thesaurus. His crack team of would-be casino robbers are straight out of central casting, but less complicated than the Ocean's Eleven ensemble. There is the dumb (almost drooling) jock, the munition expert who seems accident prone, the quick-as-a-cat Vietnamese General, and the profanity-laden gangsta who works as a janitor (the inside man).The real star of the movie is Irma Hall as a stolid black woman who has been wearing the same house dresses since her beloved husband Othar passed on to glory. Her wonderful portrayal of a Mississippian Christian woman, playing landlord to Tom Hanks and his schemes, works on many levels. I am a bigger fan of other Coen Bros. works, but this is a worthy piece of entertainment. It has clever construction, interesting use of language, and bizarre characters. It can't decide which era it belongs to but jumps between today and the 50's. My biggest complaint is the gratuitous level of cursing, but with the Coens, it can be taken as humorous instead of truly offensive. I give it a 9/10 only because I have seen other Coen movies I liked more, like Fargo and O Brother Where Are Thou.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Funny</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Despite his two Academy awards for the dreadful Philadelphia and Forrest Gump Tom Hanks has never, not once, ever sold me on his dramatic acting abilities. I remember him from that lame 1980s transvestite sitcom with the little blond dweeb who later starred on Newhart. Whenever he tries to emote Hanks just looks silly and cannot help but have a goofy grin on his face. As pallid as his dramatic ability is he is, and has always been, a gifted comedian.This talent has never been put to better use than in the most recent comedy from the Coen Brothers, The LadyKillers, which is a remake of a 1950s comedy from the U.K. that starred Alec Guinness and Peter Sellers. Having never seen the original I can only say that even were that at the comedic level of Dr. Strangelove it would not alter the fact that this is one flat-out hilarious film. Basically, it's a comedic heist film in which a sad sack troupe of gangsters tries to rip off a floating casino in Mississippi by tunneling into its vault from an old black lady's cellar. To accomplish this the ringleader, G.H. Dorr (Hanks), poses as a college professor. Along with him are a young black punk, Gawain MacSam (Marlon Wayans), a former Vietnamese general (Tzi Ma), a dopey munitions expert, Garth Pancake (J.K. Simmons- who plays J. Jonah Hameson in the Spider-Man franchise), and a big dumb lump of a football player named Lump Hudson (Ryan Hurst). The old lady, Marva Munson, is wonderfully played by Irma P. Hall.The LadyKillers is not a film for the ages, but it is one that when a little blue you can just pop it in the DVD and laugh yourself silly. That said, the DVD had a nice transfer- very crisp and in some places the film even looked like videotape. I don't know whether it was shot digitally, however. The music plays well, especially in assorted gospel scenes. There are only a few extra- standard featurettes on the film, but one extended outtakes scene that is funny- a scene where Marva is slapping Gawain silly for his use of profanity. As with everything else on this DVD it's funny as hell, and most recommended.</fullReview>

		<date>2008-08-10</date>
		<username>lawrencejohnson804</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>It's all about the Sunday service scene in the church</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I don't understand how so many of the "reviewers" here fail to see the underlying story, or symbolism, of this movie, especially since it's one of the oldest plots of all: the defeat of the scheming and wicked by the innocent and good. Everything in the movie is directed to underlining this basic story. "Mrs. Munson" is clearly innocent and simple-hearted (unaware of the irony of her fondness for Bob Jones University, for instance). The candles blow out when "Dr. Dorr" arrives--he also becomes more clearly diabolic in appearance by the end. When he dies, he hangs (for a moment) "between heaven and earth" like Judas. And in the end, he and all his accomplices are carried out to Trash Island, which is made into a symbol of hell by the preacher at the church service. Their evil activities--namely, the efforts to murder Mrs. Munson and each other--take place in the dead of night, but with the death of the Professor, it grows light again. And of course, the Gospel choir sings about the "lighthouse" and "shine your light." So, in a way, the church scene sets up the entire symbolism of the rest of the movie. Personally, I thought it was a well done film with a good blending of symbolism and plain old entertainment.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Weird and disjointed, but still quite funny</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Many of the reviewers who responded very negatively to this film where either: (A)Fans of the original and biased against a remake (B)Put off by the harsh language (C)Upset at the weirdness and wanted a more straight forward brand of comedy (D)Tom Hanks fans, expecting a more typical performance, or Tom Hanks haters who just can't enjoy anything he does.If you like a movie that's not afraid to be a little strange and eccentric, you'll probably enjoy this. The plot revolves around a gang of mismatched criminals trying to tunnel from the basement of a clueless old woman's house to the river-side vault of a riverboat casino.Tom Hanks is excellent as Professor G.H. Dorr, the ringleader. He's sort of a 1920's Southern gentleman in modern times. Or is that his disguise? He never seems to break character, so maybe he's a little crazy as well? The remainder of the heist gang is just as eccentric. There's the strangely creepy Chinese General, an expert at tunneling. The hothead, unreliable "inside man" who's a janitor on the riverboat. There's the hired muscle who might be a little mentally challenged. Finally there's the explosives "expert" who's rather clumsy.One thing that is distracting about the film is it's never clear in what decade this all takes place. Professor G.H. Dorr seems to be from the 1920's. The old, Bible-thumping African-American woman who owns the house is out of the 1950's. The working class maintenance staff of the riverboat is definitely from modern times. It seems this diverse, mismatched cast of characters was randomly picked from different movies. It takes a while to get used to, but it somehow comes together.Professor G.H. Dorr gives some wonderful rambling speeches. He's quite an expert at talking nonsense and making the gibberish sound believable to the small town rubes. When the heist goes bad, things take a murderous turn. It was enjoyable to see the inept criminals killed off one-by-one, usually by happenstance.Looking back, this movie is very hard to categorize. It's partly a heist movie, the comedy element waivers from clever to lowbrow, and the film is also a little surreal (with the strange mix of characters from different times). If you're up for something weird, eccentric and a little goofy, check it out with an open mind.</fullReview>
	</review>
