		<shortDescription>Vastly underrated film - and better than the original</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I saw the remake and then, later, watched the original.I thought the remake was better and funnier because it worked on more levels. I enjoyed the acting in both but the writing and directing were far better in the remake.I am a huge fan of the Coen Brothers and no fan of infantile potty humor. The Coens are careful listeners and lovers of American regional dialects and personalities as they showed in the movies Fargo, O Brother Where Art Thou, and Intolerable Cruelty. What some people don't seem to appreciate about "The Ladykillers" remake is that it revels in the completely different ways that each character speaks. It intentionally used totally over-the-top extremes of language from the Professor, to Gawain, Pancake, and Mrs. Munson. Given the absurdity of the plot, I think it was totally OK to push the extremes of the characters and their language. It created all kinds of great set-ups for comedy.As the situation played out and as the pressures and conflicts built, it was hilarious to see these characters trying so hard (and mostly failing!) to get through to each other. It created much bigger laughs for me than watching the British cast play it straight.I thought the character Mrs. Munson was great as a stronger (and more irritating!) personality and it's ridiculous to claim that she was somehow less vulnerable if the gang really wanted to do her in. She was every bit as oblivious to the danger she was in as she smacked Gawain around when he used "hippety hop" language.Alec Guiness, as the gang leader of the original, was a cool, collected criminal who slowly became unraveled as things went out of control. However, I thought Tom Hanks, as the Professor with no criminal experience, was just as funny as his plans went wrong and he tried so hard to use his useless academic knowledge and language, in trying to handle the gang. I loved when he tried to explain the philosophy behind the short straw to Gawain. It reminded me of a scene in the movie "The Rundown" when Chistopher Walken is trying to explain the concept of the tooth fairy to a bunch of South American lowlife criminals.I loved the whole Mountain Girl ("Swiss Miss") scene at the Waffle Hut and the the touching story of how Pancake and Mountain met at an Irritable Bowel Syndrome singles gathering in the Catskills.I didn't like the gag with the painting of the husband changing expressions but, for the most part, I found the remake to be very funny and in fact funnier when watched for the second time because so much of the humor comes from the fact that everyone is speaking a totally different language. I know that it didn't get great reviews but I can only say that I loved the writing of the movie and think it would work great as a stage play as well.I found the original to be funny but not nearly as many real laughs as the remake.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Marlon Wayans - An Obsence &amp; Unfunny Person</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I watched this movie primarily because it starred Tom Hanks. His scenes with Irma Hall as "Marva" (the landlady) provide the only highlights in this otherwise disappointing movie. I gave the movie 3 stars - one for each of the Hanks &amp; Hall enjoyable performances and another for a slight sprinkling of humor found throughout the film (for example, scenes in the Sheriff's office).The movie had great remake potential but was squandered by the foul-mouthed and unfunny Marlon Wayans. I'm not against the use of swear words in films but the foulness of his language was excessive - and not even remotely humorous. As I watched his performance, I said to myself: "Is this funny? Am I supposed to be laughing at this?"I imagine that Marlon's character was included in the film to provide contrast against the civility of the professor (Hanks) - but the result was an abysmal failure. Why ruin a movie with the inclusion of this kind of character and his obscene language? With a little more imaginative writing a genuinely funny part could have been created. "The Ladykillers" is an example of a very annoying trend in film these days - directors seem to think that they simply have to turn someone like Marlon loose and the obscene results will generate side-splitting laughs. Well, quite the opposite actually occurs. Obscene language and gestures used excessively in films such as this one are actually ugly and repulsive. The use of such language demonstrates a lack of respect both for the other characters being fictionally addressed in the film and also for the audience watching it. Many viewers unfortunately then take such obscene film language/gestures as being the norm in society and hence acceptable for them to use in their outside the theater world - but "cool" it is not.Foul-mouthed humor as presented in this film demonstrates a lack of creativity (i.e. there's nothing imaginative in using the same 4-letter words over and over again). Marlon and his foul-mouthed humor does not result in impressive screen-time. It simply is not funny and and only serves to sour any real comedy presented in the film.</fullReview>

Hall plays the uptight, highly religious woman that Hanks imposes himself on, Ms Munson. Marlon Wayans is a completely unfortunate addition to the cast. I realize the Coen Brothers are known for being a might eccentric in their film making style but I think for any average movie goer, this is taking it too far. I also recognize the odd brilliance some people will find in this film with it's small little idiosyncrasies. The one rather interesting aspect is the setting. 		<date>2004-10-19</date>
		<username>Andy (film-critic)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Don't make me wanna go hippety-hop.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When you watch a Coen brother's film, you never know what to expect. I have stated before that I normally go into these films not expecting anything. Even if I have seen the preview, with the Coens you never know if they will keep you on the Hollywood path. Luckily, they do not. The Coens are notorious (or at least becoming notorious) for making films that seem mainstream, but after careful viewing you realize that they are not. The Ladykillers is unlike any work they have done before. I may be biased because I have not seen the original version with Sir Alec Guinness, but I thought this was a very well directed film. The Coen's have not forgotten their roots and are a pair of directors that are not afraid to experiment with audiences. I was a fan of Intolerable Cruelty and pretty much every single one of their films, so I can say that with the sincerest confidence. This film was nothing short of spectacular. The Coens crafted this film using big names, small names, ingenious shots, a powerful story, and most importantly hidden messages throughout. This is no O' Brother, Where Art Thou?, but it does stand on its own two feet. I have read reviews that complain about Hanks performance in this film, but to this reviewer it was flawless. The character that he portrays is hysterical and defined. I am impressed with Hanks ability to control this character and make us become interested in him. The expressions alone on Hanks face during this film alone are worth the price of the rental or theater ticket. His banter with Marva Munson (aka Irma P. Hall) is outstanding. Never have I seen such a complete connection between these two characters. I longed for more interactions between them just so that I could witness the Coen's cinematic genius in action. Hanks couldn't have done it alone though. Thanks to Irma Hall, he has the perfect person to counter his dastardly narratives. She nearly steals the scenes from Hanks. Thankfully, their banter is equally sided and enjoyable. I can not be the only one that thinks this since Irma won top prizes at Cannes for her work in this film. It was the characters in this film that gave us the juicy center to a very tasty pie known as The Ladykillers. I have spoken about Hanks and Hall, but so much can be said about Wayans job and the rest of the crew. They each had their own defined actions and personalities that it kept my attention throughout. The great obsession about the Coens is that they take you in so many directions. From the opening scene, you assume that you have the time frame figured out, but thanks to Wayans character you are never sure when this film takes place. This film felt like a classic picture with modern day undertones. I think that was the utmost purpose of the Coens when the decided to remake this film. They didn't want to change it completely, but they wanted to just add onto it. I don't want to go too deeply into this because of my lack of knowing about the original film, but just from watching this version, you get that feeling.Secondly, I would like to add that coupled with the acting is some of the most beautiful cinematography to grace the silver screen. It plays a dual role in this film by not only setting the stage for events to come, but also helps create the very dark theme. The gargoyles, the barge, and even the police station seemed to be from a different world, giving the viewer a mythical feeling. There were times that the Coens had been reading children's books when filming this movie. There were several scenes that I felt came right from children's literature. The barge and Hanks' character are just two of the instances. I do not have much to complain about with this film. I love the Coen brother's films and I will continue to pay my money each time they release something. They are never similar and each time push the envelope of reality. I especially loved in this film the battle between 'good' and 'evil'. One could easily argue that this film was nothing more than a creative way of showing a battle between God and the Devil. There was so many spiritual references throughout the film that you couldn't help wonder if the Coens weren't trying to convert you midway through the film. Ah, I enjoyed this film. Everything from the classic picture on the wall (with changing faces), to the dimwitted burglars, to the beautiful gospel music, all the way to the over-the-top performances by Hanks and Hall, this film was nothing short of perfection. The Coens continue to make quality films that do not speak to the mass audiences, and there is nothing wrong with that. They have their own style and wit, which makes them one of the most creative directors in Hollywood!Grade: **** out of *****</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-08-15</date>
		<username>Jacob Rosen (bix171@comcast.net)</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Indulgent, Misanthropic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Tom Hanks is technically proficient, I suppose, in this update of the 1955 British comedy, but it's perhaps his most indulgent role to date; whatever humor he attempts is lost in stylized tics and windy verbiage that never seem to expire. His role as the Southern-gentleman mastermind of a riverboat casino heist should flow effortlessly but isn't particularly engaging--there's no sense of amusing malice in his Professor G.H. Dorr, though he's portrayed, unconvincingly, as some sort of absolute evil--and he doesn't seem to want to connect with much of the cast (though there is some decent interplay with his nemesis, Marva Munson, the landlady whose home he uses as his base of operation, played in over-sized fashion by Irma P. Hall, bucking for an Oscar). The upshot is that Hanks' obstinacy actually slows the rhythms of the film to a crawl and you find yourself increasingly impatient. As for the film itself, it's another empty academic exercise from the Coen Brothers, with elegant art direction by Richard Johnson and clever cinematography from Roger Deakins (the camera angles get skewed as the situation gets darkly violent) but also a misanthropic theological overview: though characters fall from grace (into a garbage barge), the heaven they're cast from is an empty, ghettoized deep-South parish that appears unclean and undesirable. They also possess a cluelessness as to what's funny, smarmily assuming jokes about irritable bowel syndrome are audience-pleasers; and their attempts at surrealist humor (most notably the varying expressions of a portrait meant to comment on the proceedings) falls dismally flat. With a sour Marlon Wayans, Tzi Ma (whose tricks with cigarettes contain the film's most entertaining moments), J.K. Simmons (from 'Spider-Man') and Ryan Hurst as Hanks' partners in crime; they're severely let down by the Coens' indifferent treatment.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Pontificating on pontification...!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The brothers Coen were once such Hollywood luminaries that, Midas-like, all they touched turned to gold, and each new release was welcomed and anticipated with certainty of it's quality. Those days ? and here I pause to wipe away a tear ? are gone. With the horrific advent of ?Intolerable Cruelty', seeds that were sown with the less-than-laudable ?The Man Who Wasn't There' flourished into utter tosh, and the Coens almost lost their infallible tag. Here, I'm afraid, the last nail is hammered home.?The Ladykillers' never quite manages to claw itself up from its raucous stereotypes ? a system that normally works (think ?Fargo' and ?The Big Lebowski'), but here only serves to highlight the films inadequacies. Tom Hanks performs admirably and, at times, with the great comic fervour he so often attacks, but something is lacking in his hissing, wheezing, slimy Professor. Irma P. Hall, lady-stalwart, is merely a gabbling southern ?momma', reminiscent of Martin Laurence's awful dress-ups, and even she cannot pull together the unravelling cords of the film. The ?motley crew' are comic relief ? at best Marlon Wayans provides his ?home-boy' jiggling, and at worst Tzi Ma fluffs schtum-faced anger. The premise ? tunnelling underground to empty a casino vault ? is well portrayed, with some fine comic turns from feline and canine alike (!). The intermittent moments of extensive dialogue, however, slow down the movie to a crawl, and allow an overall blandness to pervade the film, making it a bit of a struggle to stay awake and interested.In the end, whether they kill the ?old lady' becomes incidental, as the wish for them all to die just so they stop pontificating becomes the prevailing feeling.However, not to be all bad, the movie does provide some laugh-out-loud moments, which the cinema duly complied with, so I cannot quite write it off completely. My disappointment lies in the fact that I know the Coens can do better ? with a remake of an originally unsatisfying movie, the least they could have done was improved on the original. Peter Sellers, I'm afraid, would turn in his grave!2 out of 5 (report card effort? 'The Coens are capable of more. Must try harder next year.')</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Say it with me... "SACKBUTT"...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Tom Hanks leads a great ensemble cast in this Coen Brothers remake of the classic Ealing comedy. The Coens attempt to recast the Britishness of the original with American eccentrics, and it comes off rather well. Hanks is Professor Goldthwait Higginson Dorr, at first glance a Southern dandy, but, in fact, a man with equal weaknesses for grandiose criminal enterprises and the classics, particularly Edgar Allan Poe. He rents a room in the house of the elder church woman and widow, Mrs. Munson (Irma P. Hall), under the pretense of needing a place to rehearse a quintet of musicians on period instruments in her cellar. These are, in fact, his gang (including inside man Gawain McSam (Marlon Wayans), ex-South Vietnamese tunnel rat `The General' (Tzi Ma), demolitions expert (and I.B.S. sufferer) Garth Pancake (J.K. Simmons) and your basic classic lunkhead Lump Hudson (Ryan Hurst)). The plot this time involves a vault full of money, a riverboat casino, a tunnel, various explosives, and even a sackbutt. Yes, a sackbutt. Much rests on how well you buy Hanks'performance, but he does it for me allright! The only misfit of the cast was the cusses-like-Steve-Buscemi-in-Fargo-gangsta played by Wayans, but in the end it all well fits the dark comic template of the original, with broad slapstick and large bits of typically strange and funny Coen-esque foreshadowings. It's no Fargo or Barton Fink, but it's no shame at all either.</fullReview>

		<username>Jason Alley (samurai1978@aol.com)</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Coen brothers on autopilot (**)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen brothers are two of the most talented and unique filmmakers working these days. They have made some of the best and most interesting movies of the last 20 years ("Raising Arizona", "Fargo", "The Big Lebowski", "The Man Who Wasn't There", and the underrated "The Hudsucker Proxy"). Even their throwaway films, like "O Brother, Where Art Thou?" and "Intolerable Cruelty" are well worth watching. Now we have "The Ladykillers", and it's the only Coen brothers movie I've seen that is basically skipable (and I've seen them all except "Miller's Crossing"). That's not to say it's entirely without worth. It definitely has its great moments, but mostly it's a grating and unnecessarily crude, off-putting comedy. Most damaging is that it's clear the brothers are on autopilot, and it shows. Tom Hanks, in a rare villain role, plays a criminal mastermind named Professor G.H. Dorr, and he plays him to the hilt. It's understandable why Hanks (or any other actor) would jump at the chance of being in a Coen brothers movie, and I'm sure he had fun playing this cartoonish villain, but he's been given very few funny lines. Dorr is the most interesting character in the movie, but that's not saying much in this case.Dorr and his ragtag crew of criminals, the most interesting members being an asian man with a Hitler mustache named the General (Tzi Ma), and a demolitions expert named Pancake (J.K. Simmons from HBO's "Oz"), are renting the basement of a house owned by a religious Southern black lady (the very good Irma P. Hall). They are pretending to be a gospel music group, but actually they're planning on tunneling into an office that stores the money of a nearby Casino boat. This should make a perfectly good movie, but unfortunately, "The Ladykillers" is not a good movie. Most of the time it's smug, overly self-conscious and just not that funny (a profoundly irritating Marlon Wayans performance doesn't help). In fact, outside of the last 15 minutes (which I'll get to next), the only time I REALLY laughed was a 2 second gag about a swinging door that doesn't do what it's supposed to. Now, about those last 15 minutes. They're perfect. Honestly, if the whole movie was as enjoyable as the last 15 minutes, it would be a minor masterpiece. The story wraps up perfectly, and Dorr's grimly clever and oddly touching comeuppance is...well, it's actually got a glimmer of that old Coen brothers genius. Too bad it was nowhere to be found in the rest of the movie.</fullReview>
	</review>

This in NOT a slapstick comedy however so there needs to be explanations as to why these characters would say and do the inexplicably thoughtless things that they do. Because each of the actors does such an excellent job in creating a believable if peculiar character one simply can not suspend disbelief when they do things that not even a four year old would do.I will still look forward to the next Cohen brothers release as even a dog like this is still somewhat watchable do to the interesting characters that they create on film. 		<fullReview>The Coen brothers are among the darlings of French press specialized in cinema and throughout the years I have warmed to their quirky universe either it is "Barton Fink" (1991) and its unusual climate, this sparkling black diamond that is "Fargo" (1996) or the zany transposition of the Odyssey at the time of the Great Crisis in "O' Brother Where Art Thou" (2000).This commissioned work that was to be directed first by Barry Sonnenfield, "the Ladykillers" met lukewarm reviews in the summer 2004. True, it's a very talky movie in which sometimes too many cues trammel sincere bursts of laughter (the moment when Tom Hanks explains to Irma P. Hall the truth about his real intentions). The interest is uneven about the different installments of the plot because of the care given to them. Too much time is spent about the efforts made by the bunch to achieve their break-in and not enough time is devoted about the means undertook by the bunch of baddies to get rid of their female owner and its results. And the pure linearity of the plot is a bit too blatant.But overall this remake of a British film made in 1955 is fun to watch. This is a remake transcended in a stylish treatment, the one that typifies the Coen brothers trademark. First at the level of the story: a main character who has a somber plan in mind which will go unravel because of unexpected circumstances. Then, the Coen brothers are always on top form when they depict a gallery of like-minded weirdos. Here, a dumb Chinese general with always a cigarette between his lips, a tough football player with nothing in his head, a restless black employee of the casino, a bald technician are under the professor's command and are linked by a ramshackle alliance in which bickering and feuds brew. Laughter are really here with enormous gags which give the film accents of slapstick comedy. This vivid description is doubled with a sharp satire of America imbued with religious values. The casino is located in Sodome and the river is called the Styx.It would also be difficult to resists Tom Hanks who is irresistible as the elegant, mean professor keen on ancient languages and also the brain of the operation. All these assets constitute a solid, defensive alibi to justify the change of scenery. The filmmakers also capture the vibe and mentality of the people who are rooted in the Deep South. So, this oddball overhaul exudes an effervescent, communicative treat and to deem the Coen brothers' 2004 vintage as a miscarriage is exaggerated. Perhaps not as indispensable as "Fargo" or "the Big Lebowski" (1998) but worth a watch all the same.</fullReview>

I think of this film as another chapter in the ever growing book of wonderful films that will be the Coen Brothers' legacy. 		<shortDescription>robbery comedy in the American South</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I find myself disagreement with many of the IMDb viewers who saw and commented on this film, and with many of the critics. I liked the film, and I cannot imagine why other did not. The film is based on an original British comedy in the 50s staring Alec Guinness and Peter Sellers, which I did not see. The story is being transposed to the American South, and without reaching really the picks of 'O, Brother!' the Coen brothers did not land too far. This is a crime story with a gathering of criminals renting a room in an old black American lady home just to establish a base for a casino robbery from the basement of the building. They pretend to be Renaissance music players, although all of them with the exception of the head of the gang look like persons not able to spell properly neither Renaissance, nor music. Tom Hanks is their leader, and the fine actor enjoys himself in the bad guy role, playing the erudite university professor, combining a Southern accent with the charm of a Bernard Shaw character quoting from Edgar Allan Poe. Of course, as the film develops, the old lady will over-wit the criminals plans in a story that has nothing to do with real life logic, but has a lot to do with comedy logic. The film is filmed with style, well acted, and the comic of characters and of language is visible almost in any moment. A special note should be made for the musical score, the Coen brothers gathering again a collection of Southern gospel music, in a collection which makes me want to go and look for the soundtrack CD at the first opportunity. I enjoyed the film, it's an escapist show and not more, but not less either - and a well done one. 8 out of 10 on my personal scale.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Coens miss the joke</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Sometimes it only takes a single mistake to destroy a movie. The joke in The Ladykillers is a very simple one: namely, that a sweet tiny little old lady who lives in a world of her own and who wouldn't hurt a fly not only thwarts a gang of criminals who want to kill her and ends up with their ill-gotten gains but does it without even knowing it. Unfortunately, the Coens just didn't seem to get it and make one disastrous change in their remake: Mrs Lopsidey is now a huge, loud, obnoxious, hateful and extremely violent black woman who you want dead on sight. Not only do the crooks never stand a chance, but within seconds of her first scene you have no desire to watch a minute more.Things improve slightly when Tom Hanks' loquacious decayed Southern gentleman appears, but he's fighting a losing battle with her spite and vindictiveness. They then compound the error by casting Marlon Wayans, whose ability to spout obscenities is almost miraculously untouched by the ability to make any of them remotely funny. Thus the stage is set for the Coen's worst movie, and one that alternates too few genuinely funny scenes ? most involving J.K. Simmons and Tzi Ma's early scenes before the script run out of good ideas for them ? with moments of pure torture (the less said about Ryan Hurst's painfully misfired performance as the dumb lummox of the gang the better). By the time the miscreants finally put their minds to lady killing, the Coens have long since run out of ammunition, making for a flat, unfunny and painfully unimaginative last half-hour.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>In "The Ladykillers", an erudite and refined Southern Gentleman (Hanks) takes a room with an elderly but sagacious black women in MIssissippi for the purpose of using her basement to tunnel to a vault full of gambling money with assistance from a group of four men, all stereotypical caricatures, who pretend to be an ensemble of musicians. A not-for-everyone comedy which is long on style and short on story, "The Ladykillers" is more likely to elicit the occasional cluck of the tongue than boisterous guffaws. 		<username>TheFanReport</username>
		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>Hanks Takes the Coen Brothers Hostage</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have loved all the Coen brother movies but, unfortunately, this one was a let down. It kills me to write this but here is my analysis of what went wrong...The Actors &amp; Production Team did not Share the Same Vision.......and it shows on screen. The tone of the scenes and characters are wildly uneven. The movie feels like it was pasted together from other movies of unrelated genres and time periods.The odd combinations of mixed era costumes and set interiors are visually distracting. It takes awhile to figure out what time period the story is supposed to be set in. When introduced to Tom Hank's character it appears the movie will be set in the 1920's but then you meet his landlady, and she is an artifact of the 1950's. Then, suddenly, you are jerked to a casino scene that feels like outtakes from an Ice Cube "Friday" movie. The only cohesive element is that all the characters are underdeveloped stereotypes. Typically the Coens write quirky characters that are meant to be played full tilt boogie but here lack of a developed script leaves the actors running amok. Although the actors try to compensate their overwrought performances just amplify the offensiveness of the humorless material. Hanks' performance as Professor Dorr might have been fun in a short SNL sketch but his over-the-top delivery is hard to take for longer than five minutes. His mugging and affectations are blown even more out of proportion when viewed in the theater (or on a big screen). It is quite like watching the antics of a precocious kid who annoys everyone but his love-blind parents. While Hanks may have amused the Coen brothers, it isn't working on scene. The story might have looked funny on paper but by the time it reached the big screen Tom Hanks had taken the Coen Brother's hostage. Hanks character interpretation looks like it evolved in a bubble, cut off from his fellow actors and unrelated to the stylistic tone of the surrounding production. The effect is jarring and frustrating to watch. The Step'in Fetchit and Mammy era shtick is painfully unfunny. The casino scenes lack the usual Coen whimsy. Visually it feels like you accidentally clicked over to another movie. If you are looking for the usual Coen Brothers whimsy you may wish to skip this one and save yourself the disappointment. I can recommend this movie ONLY to people that enjoy dogging on movies that missed the mark. In that sense it is a good candidate for the at-home game of "Mystery Science Theater 3000." Invite over a few friends and swap smartass comments about movie while you view it.</fullReview>

		<date>2012-06-10</date>
		<username>faterson (faterson@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Funnier &amp; Better Directed than the Original</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I don't hold the original 1955 version of _The Ladykillers_ in high esteem (and I explain in my review of it why I don't), so my expectations when approaching the Coen brothers' version of _The Ladykillers_ were suitably lowered. Judging by that, I was pleasantly surprised by the experience. This means I got the opposite experience compared to watching the original version: because the original is praised as a masterpiece, I was disappointed by it; not so here. The main thing I bemoaned about the 1955 version of _The Ladykillers_ was its ostentatious lack of realism; for me, I think this began right at the moment when Herbert Lom entered the widow's house wielding his violin as a machine-gun; yet no one can possibly be as dumb as that; with that moment, all semblance to the real world went out the window; it became clear that here was a group of actors trying to do funny things in order to excite laughs from the audience. And in that sense, the 1955 version was successful -- but only moderately so, in my estimation. Now, in the Coen brothers' version, we get the much-needed shift towards and infusion of realism; yes, the 5 gangsters are buffoons just like they are in the original British version -- but they are, at the same time, menacing creatures. The altercations between the risible characters of Garth Pancake and Gawain MacSam (fabulously portrayed by J. K. Simmons and Marlon Wayans) are as real as in any dramatic gangster movie (from the Coens or any other film-maker). And that's nothing compared to the menace exuding from "The General" played by Tzi Ma; I think this is one hallmark of great art: to succeed in portraying something as both funny and menacing at the same time; the character of "The General" is a 100% success in this regard. The transition from London to the Mississippi region succeeds splendidly; the changes and modifications in how the culprits meet their fate are all intelligent and fun to watch; the widow's character is less central in this version than it is in the British version, but Irma P. Hall is thoroughly believable in the role. The gospel-dominated "Southern" soundtrack, once more masterminded by T-Bone Burnett, is as delightful here as it was previously in the Coens' masterpiece _O Brother, Where Art Thou?_. Another shift was made to the "professor" character: Tom Hanks plays him as a far more literary and intellectual person (particularly in his diction) than Alec Guiness's professor in the 1955 version; in Alec Guiness's case, it is all too obvious that this is an impostor masquerading as a professor; whereas Hanks recites Poe so convincingly one might actually believe that that -- professor -- was his original vocation. The cat is, I believe, funnier here than the parrot(s) in the original version; dedicating the final scene of the movie to the cat and the macabre thing it holds in and releases from its mouth -- that is a quintessential Coenesque touch. So is the profanity; in most other films, you might be disturbed by it, but here, the screenwriters (Coens themselves) manage to interlace the profanity with the action so that it makes perfect sense. The addition of characters and events such as "Mountain Girl" and "IBS Singles Weekend" are as ingenious and hilarious as one would expect from the brothers. All in all, _The Ladykillers_ as remade by the Coens is no masterpiece -- there's too little substance in the story for that, I'm afraid -- but it delivers fine, solid entertainment.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Quirky, amusing black comedy is set up like an undistinguished caper film but elevated by sharp writing, colorful characters, and lively performances. Marva Munson is a devout southern widow who advertises a room for rent. It's quickly obtained by Professor G.H. Dorr (Hanks), an eccentric, self-proclaimed musician and gentleman who's really the ringleader of a zany criminal ensemble, which include a demolitions expert with an erratic case of irritable bowel syndrome, a lewd, foulmouthed man on the inside, a taciturn killer, and a bumbling meathead. Together they plan to pull off a lucrative casino heist, but complications ensue when Marva grows suspicious and their presumably perfect scheme hits some major detours. Effective twists, good lines, and witty, laugh-out loud funny slapstick (along with an energetic cast) make this a lot of fun. Hanks is broadly funny in a campy, cartoonish role as the silver-tongued, duplicitous Dorr, and Hall is a hoot as a sassy southerner. ***</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I do not often review films, but a reading of the reviews on this site dating mostly from the time of the release spurred me to add my two cents. Each of these sittings revealed new lines, more nuances of acting and direction lying just beneath the surface of what is, as others have already noted here, a simple story.Parodoxically, a movie filled with profanity and toilet humor may have failed in the eyes of many because it is too literate and too subtle. I also suspect many of the naysayers for this Ladykillers remake set in the Deep South are partisans of that region. A Mencken-like satire of southern social and religious institutions ("The State of Mississippi has, in its infinite wisdom, declared that no wagering take place upon its shores") and a mocking of liberal hubris (Garth Pancake's Freedom Rider speech) probably helped to ensure that a large portion of the audience below the Mason Dixon were offended. Added to this are the usual folks everywhere who would have movie language reflect an earlier, more gentile time. Like many Coen Brothers brothers films, this effort seems to appeal more to the guys thus making it even more specialized--a guy flick which relies more upon timing and dialog than it does on action and storyline. Tom Hanks' excellent portrayal of a half-learned, anachronistic sociopath is one of his best in my opinion but my lady friend of the time found him too creepy and smarmy, adding perhaps a further, unintended nuance to the title.</fullReview>

		<date>2005-12-05</date>
		<username>Ezagouri</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A quiet masterpiece from the Coens, and yet, not quite, if I may offer a riddle...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As is well known, the British can be rather protective of their "classics" (Alfie, Italian Job, Get Carter) so when it was announced that there would be an American remake of the classic Ealing black comedy Ladykillers, the critics obviously got a little, shall we say, snappy? Admittedly, there didn't seem to be any need to remake the original which was near-perfect and in no need of an updating. Fair enough, I can understand the frustration, and yet?I can't. For this is no regular schmo taking on the remake, this is the Coen Brothers' show. And when geniuses are at work, it's often better to shut up and let them do their stuff.The film starts off with a masterful, very dark intro as we descend onto a spooky gargoyle on which a raven is perched and way down below under the bridge, rests the Mississippi river where a boat is carrying trash over to the nearby garbage island. After that promising, visually inspired start, we follow Mrs Munson (Hall), an old righteous African American woman who lives by herself in a big, pretty house. Her peaceful routine is soon disturbed by the unexpected arrival of a strange, snobbish man who calls himself: Professor Gothwaite Higginson Dorr Phd (Hanks). From the moment he darkens her door, it's obvious he's up to something. With his geeky laugh, his insincere snaky smile and his sophisticated manners he starts to dazzle the innocent Mrs Munson with conversation and explains to her why he has come: he is looking to rent one of her rooms and he needs the root cellar in order to practice the music of the Renaissance from the Rococo with his "ensemble" (whatever that means). Or at least that's what he claims. Truth is, he is planning to dig a tunnel in Mrs Munson's cellar with his team of eccentric losers in order to get to the casino offices where all the money lies.In the team is Clark Pancake (Simmons): a strong-ish guy with a bad case of IBS and for whom every situation, no matter how hopeless it seems, can be resolved with a minimum of fuss: "easiest thing in the world!". The others include a quiet, serious Vietnamese man called The General (Ma) whose specialities include tunnelling and hiding cigarettes on his agile tongue. Gawain (Wayans) is the "inside man" of the group as he works at the casino. And of course there's Lump (Hurst), a brainless football player who's only there to provide the muscles. Everything seems to go well but have they underestimated the "harmless" Mrs Munson? When one day she finds out their little game (sort of) it all gets even darker and the group have to get rid of her which proves to be much easier said than done.It's a pity this film was so underrated upon its release because there's a lot to admire in it. Such as Tom Hanks' show-stealing incarnation of the sneaky, Edgar Allan Poe-reciting Professor (pretty much Alec Guinness' role in the original). It's a welcome return to comedy for Hanks which reminds us how terrifically funny he can be, it's a devilish performance which surely deserved an Oscar nomination. The rest of the cast is great, except perhaps for Marlon Wayans' increasingly irritating performance: he is clearly miscast, which is a shame because this creates an unwelcome weak link in the chain. This is a film that's not only very funny but also visually slick and perfectly executed not to mention brilliantly written (Ethan and Joel Coen really surpass themselves with the Professor's pompous dialogs) and very well acted. Having said that, it is probably the Coens' "less good" film (which is impressive in itself) and it is slightly flawed in that a few changes to the original film don't really work. Such as the old woman herself, whereas in the Ealing comedy, she was frail and vulnerable, here, she looks like she could take these bozzos on easily, so there's no real danger present, we know their plan is doomed from the start. Thankfully though, Hall is so good you barely notice the problem. Also, there's a lot of vulgar and crude humour which occasionally works but doesn't quite fit into the old-fashioned setting (hence the miscasting of the abhorrent Wayans). Other than that and a few other minor glitches, it's perfect: a nasty, gloomy, yet clever and very funny black comedy.Overall, the Coens prove once again that they haven't lost their magic touch and that they can effortlessly provide fine films which rise way above the average comedies we get these days (Mr and Mrs Smith my arse!). Wrongfully underrated and well worth all your attention. The Ladykillers is certainly an under-appreciated (if slightly flawed) little gem. I love it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Why, I can scarcely contain my glee</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Most deplorable is the fact that I have yet to obtain the assumed pleasure of seeing the original, so what follows may be distorted, for I have no emotional attachments. (Was there a contradiction there?) 		<date>2005-07-04</date>
		<username>Kieran Kenney</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Not the Cohen Brothers' best by any measure, but still head and shoulders above the rest.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The premise of the original Ladykillers, the 1955 British film, was a clever one, but it had been made into such and ungodly dull and awkward film that it was practically begging to be re-envisioned. In an age where it seems like Hollywood is producing nothing but remakes, this one was handed, for once, to a pair who could really do it justice.Joel and Ethan Cohen, the writer-director pair who have made several masterpieces over the past twenty years, have produced a film that at least looks and feels commercially viable. It has humor, characters and visuals that everyone can appreciate. It moves at a steady pace, the snappy dialog carried swiftly along by an outstanding soundtrack, and is aided in no small part by Dennis Gassner's excellent production design, and by Roger Deakons' gorgeous and vivid camera-work.The sensibility that the Cohen Brothers bring to each of their films is definitely a unique one. Below the humor on the surface, there is something more sinister (and infinitely more humorous) at work. Take for instance the character of Marva Munson (Irma P. Hall), a nice old black lady, a widow with a cat, and her husband's portrait above the mantle. She writes a check every month to a nice bible school named Bob Jones University, a university that has a history of discrimination against blacks. Or take the 'fine colored by' Gawain (Marlon Wayans) who doesn't know anything about the Freedom Riders and, when annoyed by a veteran of the civil rights movement, says 'just tell me when they gonna leave!' Garth Pancake (J.K. Simmons) reminds him that it was so people like him could vote, Gawain says he doesn't vote and tells him to where to stick it.By the way, the casino where Gawain works (the target for our 'merry band of miscreants') is staffed entirely by black workers, who are apparently cheaper to hire in the south than whites. When Gawain is laid off, he threatens to sue for punitive damages. His white boss reminds him that 'everyone on the custodial staff is black. Your replacement is gonna be black. His replacement not doubt will be BLACK!'Rather than just accepting this as the norm, the Cohens find humor and intrigue in these situations, and the astute viewer will notice this. Looking past the surface of this movie, which one might call 'zany' or 'farcical', there's some interesting social comment being made about the US and the south.As for the other components of the movie, they are all well assembled. Tom Hanks is great as Professor Dorr. Perfect (off-type) casting has lead to one of the best performances of the year. Hanks is having great fun with this role. The other parts are played just as well. Simmons is great as the self-assured, self-righteous Mr. Pancake, Ryan Hurst is adorable as the thick-headed Lump, and Tsi Ma makes a memorable impression as the silent General. Marlon Wayans is kept in check for once, and demonstrates that he can be tolerated, and actually be very funny, when directed by somebody other than himself or his brothers. As for Irma Hall, she fits her role like a glove. Also very memorable is Stephen Root as the casino boss. The photography is particularly gorgeous and inventive for a comedy; you can never go wrong with Roger Deakins. Despite not being the best film the Cohen brothers have done, this is an excellent and very interesting comedy that I recommend highly.</fullReview>
	</review>

His character was just a little too confrontational to have survived long enough.3. A few odd scenes here and there which were just head-scratching. For example, after being bullied into a music recital by the old lady, Hank's politely declines as Wayan's character isn't there to accompany them. When, a few minutes later, the Wayans returns, was I the only one to expect an awful music rendition from the group? Apparently so, as the scene cuts to the ladies leaving the house with not even the hint of any music having been played. What went on there?All in all, Ladykillers is an OK film, but nothing to write home about. The long moments of southern-drawl inconsequential dialogue from Hanks are easily tuned out without the film losing anything in the slightest. Gien this, it's a wonder that they are there at all. Likewise the real laughs are few and far between, making them all the more precious when they do occur.This is a flawed movie that could have been so much better with a couple of cast changes and a lot less meaningless dialogue. As it is, it's a watch-once affair.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>a fantastic remake, and a brilliant Coens film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I loved the original Ladykillers. Yet no film is sacrosanct, you are always entitled to re-imagine the story. Remakes are possible. There needs to be no justification. But a remake often falls in the trap of being nothing more than just a remake of another film. This one isn't. It's both a remake and a Coens film.Transplanting the story to Mississippi is a bold move, but it works. Mrs Munson is not Mrs Wilberforce, but she's just as lovely. And Tom Hanks surely can meet the standards established by Alec Guinness. In fact, there are some things the remake does even better than the original. There is more character exposition, more room given for development, and more concentration on details. The original was more heads-on to the story, this film actually depicts the world it talks about.And how it is done! The attention to detail, the minute observations, the overall love for the characters, and for the characters' goofiness, as if beams of light would grow out of the screen. Unmistakably a Coens film, to every inch.There's just one problem: You have to watch the film with an open heart. That's how it was made. Otherwise, you probably won't give a darn.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-08-06</date>
		<username>MurrayFan</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A well-executed farce that oozes Gothic charm</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Based on the Ealing Comedy of the same name, The Ladykillers is the latest film from the ever-reliable Coen brothers. Tom Hanks plays Professor Goldthwait Higginson Dorr PhD, a loquacious charlatan who takes a room in the house of devout Christian widow, and professional busybody Mrs Munson. Little does she know, however, that Professor Dorr and his gang of miscreants are using her basement to tunnel into a casino's poorly guarded cash room, under the guise of being a practising renaissance music ensemble. As is so often the case in these situations, Mrs Munson discovers the plot, and it is the gang's only option to dispose of her. After all, how hard can it be to kill one little old lady? This film marks a welcome return to comedy for Hanks after years in worthy but dull everyman parts. They may win him Oscars, but you can tell from the moment we meet Professor Dorr, with his immaculate devil beard, higgledy-piggledy teeth and snorting giggle of a laugh that he's never had this much fun playing a role. Hanks utterly steals the show, oozing an uncomfortable oily charm as he does so. The rest of the cast are uniformly excellent as well, as Professor Dorr assembles his gang from the typical movie assortment of comically mismatched criminals. J.K Simmons (Spider-man's J. Jonah Jameson) stands out in particular as a gruff explosive expert with an improbable mustache, but Marlon Wayans as an antagonistic homeboy and Ryan Hurst as an incredibly stupid American football player help round out the gang nicely.The relocation of the film to modern day Mississippi also allows the Coen Brothers to heavily tinge the story with the kind of Southern Gothic that they do so well. The streets are filled with mist, hideous gargoyles with screaming mouths look down on the events taking place, and the boats heading toward the city's Garbage Island are imbued with the grim finality of Charon's boat crossing the river Styx . The gang's preferred method of transport is even a hearse. However, it is against this Gothic backdrop that the Cohen's allow a finely executed farce to develop. Professor Dorr's eloquent wordplay, bringing to mind George Clooney's character from O Brother Where Art Thou, is hilarious in itself, and the bickering between the gang, and the devout Christian outrage of their landlady toward just about everything (especially "that damn hippity-hop music"), make for some genuinely excellent comic moments. There are a few gags that feel out of place, or fail to hit the mark (incongruously the painting of Mrs Munson's dead husband above the fireplace changes its facial expression in most scenes in reaction to what is going on), but the Coens create a cinematic world that is so involving and so enthralling that such misgivings fall by the wayside. Doubtless there will be cries of dissent from fans of the original film, but when a film is simply this fun to watch, such criticism can be easily ignored.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>an improvement on a brilliant original</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The original film was a delightful entertainment, sending up the chaotic but mysterious atmosphere of postwar London (spoiler coming) and topping the whole charade off with poetic justice as the bad guys got theirs. The Coens' version delivers just as much fun, some marvelous jokes (You shitting me? to the guy with IBS)and crackerjack performances, notably Hanks doing a far more villainous take on the Professor than Alec Guiness with the ill-fitting false teeth, but it also carries a more thoughtful moral message. The old lady is given a much richer part to play, more reflective, more significant. Alexander McKendrick in the original left you thinking that after all there was no moral centre; the Coens set Marva Munson in a clearly conceived moral universe where justice is no longer merely poetic but actual and a product of the individual's own moral values. The cat was an inspiration, too. A splendid film.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I haven't seen the original Ealing version of this comedy, so I have no point of reference to it. I have however witnessed the majority of the Coen brothers' films, and so my viewpoint is relative to these films.Before I saw The Ladykillers, I read a few reviews of the film, some bad, the majority good, none brilliant, so I thought it was be a wise decision to enter the auditorium telling myself not to expect too much.The first few minutes of the film are a bit slow. I was eagerly waiting for the entrance of Tom Hanks, and stuck with the Luke-warm antics of Irma P. Hall talking to a portrait, I wondered if the film would be a total let-down. I mean, people were claiming the Coens were losing it. One particular review I read called The Man Who Wasn't There disappointing and Intolerable Cruelty 'frankly poor'. I did, however, enjoy Intolerable Cruelty, and loved The Man Who Wasn't There, in fact, I tore up the paper containing the review on the spot. Regardless, this film seemed slightly lack-lustre. Reviews kinda shakey, and their previous film not really living up to their others' standards, maybe the Coens were running out of talent.But then, just as I was ready to give up all hope, I see Tom Hanks standing at the door, dressed in a gray overcoat, armed with a mouth that could talk the tail off a donkey. His performance was, undoubtedly brilliant, perfect as the phoney Professor Goldthwait Higginson Dorr PhD.To be fair, the whole cast is brilliant. J.K Simmons (recognisable from Spider-Man as The Daily Bugle's editor, Mr Jameson) is great as the IBS awareness raising, blundering explosives expert, Garth Pancake; Marlon Wayans is pretty good considering some of the other things hes been involved in. Tzi Ma fulfills as 'The General' a quiet but bold man, dangerous with only his bare hands, and Ryan Hurst as lump. Not a particularly demanding role, but he plays it pretty well anyway, and is first scene in the film is great. Even Irma P. Hall pleases, despite my first impressions, more than holding her own pretty much whenever shes on screen.As I'm sure you know, the basic run-down of the story goes like this; Professor G.H. Dorr plans to rob a casino, and employs a team to help him in his plan. He bases their activity in the basement of a house belonging to an aged woman named Marva Munson. He plans to tunnel from this basement to the holding room of the casino and collect its wealth. The whole plan goes well (enough) until Marva finds out about their little scheme, and they're forced to either kill her, or to give up the money. Just around here is when people begin dying, and we're barely given time to breathe between each death. The cast seems so die away so fast, that only the first death makes an impression; the others aren't given enough space to themselves, and so we don't really get the time to contemplate each one.Basically, this film is far from a masterpiece, but its definitely a good 1 hour 45 minutes worth of entertainment. Just don't expect too much, and you should enjoy it.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Enjoyable enough, but Minor Coen work</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As a big fan of the Coens, I admit I was a little hesitant to watch this film on the big screen but I'm glad I did. Dorr Phd, and the Coens clearly haven't lost their knack for some wonderful dialogue. However Alec Guiness is much more unusual in the original, exuding a touch more menace; as if Nosferatu went to public school or something. Many people have complained that the inclusion of various bottom-related jokes are just there for an easy laugh. Well, yes, but the original had some fairly broad and unsubtle slapstick humour too, such as the scene with Frankie Howard's fruit seller. Yes, the Coens are going for a cheap laugh but why not. They are quite funny and one running gag regarding IBS has a decent payoff.Despite all this the film still has some genuinely funny moments and I had me chuckling quietly throughout. Tom Hanks, as mentioned before, is a delight to watch, and the rest of the "ensemble" are all pretty much one-joke characters, but they all have their moments. I particularly enjoyed some of Lump's facial expressions as he attempts to follow the Professor's wonderfully meandering "double-talk", as Mrs Munson describes it and the POV introduction to his character.Roger Deakins' cinematography didn't particularly catch my eye as in other Coen films considering the distinctive Southern location, but their were a couple of nice shots and some amusing cartoon-style whip pans and the ever-changing face of Othar Munson. Carter Burwell's music however was very good, in fact a few vague similarities in his various scores for the Coens was one of the few things that reminded me I was watching one of their films. I think many people have overrated the gospel soundtrack, it's nowhere near as good as T-Bone Burnetts work on "O Brother..." but it fits Mrs Munson's character and the general setting well.Verdict: Comparisons with the original aside, this is fairly forgettable but is great fun while it lasts. Fans of the Coens may still be left wanting more, but I found it more entertaining than their previous effort "Intolerable Cruelty". Hopefully the Coens will be back with more material in the league of "Lebowski" and "Barton Fink" but for now the whimsical "The Ladykillers" is worth a look. Rating: Tricky - an 8 when I left the cinema, maybe a 7 in retrospect.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A decent film, but an ingenious Tom Hanks</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Frankly, I wasn't all that impressed with the original British version. There. I've said it. I thought it had a lacklustre script with poorly drawn characters. The Coen Brothers, on the other hand, have the exact opposite problem. The script isn't much better, but they're so fixated on delineating all five of their criminals into easily identifiable types that they've left realism behind in the dirt and gone for effect alone. And for my money, this approach is the better one for a medium-grade comedy.The one true stroke of genius here is the characterization of Professor Goldthwait Higginson Dorr, Ph.D. He's a character of seemingly limitless vocabulary and infinite lung capacity, and is not afraid to use both. He resembles nothing so much as an evil Colonel Sanders. Tom Hanks takes the ball and runs with it, giving him a style of address that's an odd mixture menace, affability, and condescension, not to mention a bizarrely obsequious laugh that seems to come from a nine-year old. This sole character is worth the price of admission.The thing that's always confused me about this movie is how everyone always assumes G.H. Dorr is only posing as a professor of the Greek and Roman classics. I'm sure he really is one. Only a man who has learned his Gorgias VERY well could conceivably have such mastery over the arts of rhetoric and sophistry.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>a review</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen brothers are back. This was the feeling I was filled with as I rose from my seat upon seeing their latest release a remake of the 1955 film `The Ladykillers'. Back from where though? It was only last year that their previous film `Intolerable Cruelty' was released. A film that, in my humble opinion, was misunderstood. In everyway this George Clooney and Catherine Zeta-Jones vehicle was a charming film going experience. Certainly not the dud that most purported it to be. But for whatever reason it lacked that `Coen Brother feeling'(Barton Fink is obviously my favorite Coen outing). With `The Ladykillers' the feeling is back and so to many it will seem the brothers are back.Let me state that I have never seen the 1955 comedy on which this version is based. That said let me say I found this film to be enormously funny. From beginning to end the laughs don't stop. Comedy is about timing, period. And this film is so well paced. From its outrageous performances to the rock solid script, it's all right on time. The films comedy is simultaneously outrageous and subtle. The Coen's don't waist a moments screen time. The subtlety comes from how well everything is woven together, how every moment pays off either right away or later. Mr. Hanks as Professor G.H. Dorr is superb and provides much of the afor mentioned outragousness. He gives a performance so convincing you honestly forget this is Tom Hanks, cliché, yes, but completely true. His very presence makes you laugh. If it dosnt you should demand your money back because you wont enjoy this film. J.K Simmons (Spiderman's J. Jonah Jamison) is brilliant as the films `Jack of all trades Garth Pancake. Like Mr. Hanks he seems to enhabit his character so fully as to completely convince you that there is no performance at all. Nothing new here from Marlon Wayans who is funny as usual and in this film doing essentially what he is best known for. Rodger Deakins beautiful eye for all things cinemagraphic once again teams with the brothers for the 8th time. And thank goodness for that. His style is completely without flaw and totally suits this portrayal of the American south. When I think about the cinematography of this movie I think symmetry and repetition. Repetition is also comedy and the Coen's know this. Why else repeat the trash barge shot so many times?So basically.yeah, I liked this flick. The sleight disappointment of Intolerable Cruelty(and I do mean sleight) is now quelled.</fullReview>

Hall delivers a wonderful performance as the unwitting host of Hanks' criminal enterprise, Allowing them access to her root cellar for their "rehearsal," unaware that they are actually secretly tunneling into an underground vault. Simmons gives us the bathroom humor (where would we be without it?) he has a disease called IBS, which basically consists of random bursts of flagellance at various points in the film.So, is this film a pretty good comedy delivering some good laughs? Yes. Is it a Coen Brothers film though? No. So where are the Coens? You're guess is as good as mine. And while some things about this movie scream Joel and Ethan,They remain decidedly AWOL throughout. Maybe someday they'll come home to their former glory, but right now, they are definitely out of their element.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-04-11</date>
		<username>ilovedolby</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>The Coens have crafted a dark comedy that although not their best work and ludicrous in story, remains to be a laugh-riot</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Some think that Ethan and Joel Coen films have become trivial considering their former successes with `Raising Arizona' and `Fargo.' Their previous film `Intolerable Cruelty' was nothing special, but still a humorous romantic romp. Their current film, `The Ladykillers,' is a remake of a 1955 film of the same title. This new version stars Tom Hanks and it is a better comedy than `Intolerable Cruelty.' Perhaps one of the reasons that critics are starting to look down on the Coens is that their current releases are more of a simple breed-but one cannot conclude that the Coens are making bad movies. Sometimes filmmakers do not want to break new ground-they simply want to make a movie. With `The Ladykillers,' the Coens have crafted a dark comedy that although not their best work and ludicrous in story, remains to be a laugh-riot. `The Ladykillers' tells the story of Marva Munson, a widow with impeccable values living in a small Southern community. One morning she hears a knock at her door and a man, resembling that of a young Colonel Sanders, is standing there. His name is Professor G.H. Dorr. He speaks with a Southern drawl, has a great deal of intellect and he strikes Ms. Munson as somewhat unusual. Wanting nothing more than to rent a room, she informs the Professor that she is looking for a quiet tenet. His response is, `Madame, you are addressing a man who is quiet and yet?not quiet.' What Ms. Monson does not realize is that the Professor is a criminal mastermind and has plans of his own. The widow has a root cellar and if tunneled through properly, it could lead to an underground counting room of a gaming ship docked nearby to Ms. Monson's home. The professor tricks his new landlord into believing that his visiting friends are non other than musicians and they are practicing music in her basement. But the heist will not be easy-Ms. Monson is a curious old lady and no matter how the men try to allude her, she always ends up in their way. The real scene-stealer of the movie is not Hanks-rather Irma P. Hall who plays Ms. Monson. Her portrayal of a holier than thou old lady is excellent. At the film's beginning, she makes a trip to the local police station where she complains to the sheriff about what she calls, `hippity-hop music with the titles spelled all funny.' Moreover, one of the Professor's accomplices is called Gawain, played by Marlon Wayans. He begins to curse in Ms. Monson's presence and since she is a devout Christian, she will not allow that kind of language in her home. She begins to slap Gawain repeatedly claiming that she tries to smite the wickedness out of him. Beyond Ms. Monson, the Professor himself is hilarious to watch as he constantly refers to Edgar Allen Poe quotes and manages to come up with new and amazing ways to trick Ms. Monson into believing he is someone that he is not. Furthermore, his gang is the funniest combination that has been seen on film so far this year. There is an explosives expert named Garth who suffers from irritable bowel syndrome, a former Chinese General who owns a donut shop, a bad football player named Lump, and a cocky insider into the gaming ship named Gawain. All in all, `The Ladykillers' is nothing remarkable, but it is certainly a funny film to watch that will leave you rolling in the aisles of the theater. ***</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>mrtanner</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The Language is Magnificent!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>So much offense taken at the "language" in this film. One of the things that I most loved about it and found most remarkable was the language...ALL of it. From the elegant, erudite Tom Hanks to the old- time Southern Irma P. Hall, to the vulgar Marlon Wayans, to the plainspoken J.K. Simmons, to the monosyllabic Ryan Hurst to the taciturn Tzi Ma. Each character had his/her own manner of speaking that defined their particular character. That is what good dialogue is all about. Far from being offended by Marlon Wayans, I was in awe of how his character was as much a master of "gutter" language as Tom Hanks' character was of high-blown rhetoric, and found the contrast between the two to be hilarious. All too often movie dialogue is seen as secondary to plot and character. Finally, a film where the characters don't all sound alike and sometimes don't sound like anything I've heard before! Thank God for the Coen Brothers, who realize how inextricably dialogue is intertwined with character and continue to amaze me with the way they work AND play with language.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>I laughed my head off!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The other night I went to see the latest version of a 1955 classic, "The Ladykillers," starring Tom Hanks and Irma P. Hall. I had no idea this movie was so funny. I practically laughed my head off in the theater. A few other audience members were likewise laughing like crazy at all of the wild antics going on between a fake music professor and his cronies, and a fine, senior Christian lady who also becomes their landlady of sorts. I did not approve of all of the profanity, which did not contribute very much to the general story line, but I did like the overall screenplay structure, based on the original Oscar-nominated British screenplay by William Rose. The characters, with all of their idiosyncrasies, bear certain traits which are key to the plot. I believe the entire cast truly enjoyed themselves on the set, and must have found it very hard to keep a straight face while the cameras were rolling. I must thank brothers Joel and Ethan Coen for concocting such a wise, zany, and satisfying adaptation such as this new version of "The Ladykillers." When you mix elements such as the Old South with contemporary times, grand theft, classical and gospel music, innocence with deceit, and an expressive portrait of a deceased gentleman, something unusual and crazy is bound to happen. Even without having seen the original movie, this remake gave me a pretty good idea of how well the original version was written and acted. For a great time at the movies, I recommend that you go and see how funny, yet touching "The Ladykillers" really is. If you like black comedy, in the vein of "Brazil" and "American Beauty," then "The Ladykillers" is definitely up your alley.</fullReview>

		<username>sallyfifth</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>We're on a waterway to HELL!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There is an inevitability about the Ladykillers that I like. The films opens with shots of an island of garbage and a slow moving barge that ferries the trash from the mainland out. And like that barge you can sort of see where everything is headed, it's the genius of the film that it somehow still keeps things interesting. Forget that this is a revisioning of an older film, as the similarities between the two are mostly starting points for the Coens much the way they claim a movie of Homer's Odyssey was the starting point for "Oh, Brother! Where art thou?". Irma P. Hall and Tom Hanks are pure creations and they make the movie. Hanks is the kind of charming but despicable rat that you'll cringe with as he's put in progressively more awkward situations, but you'll never want to cheer him on or see him win. Which is important because the movie has a moral center upon which the comedy and pathos depends. The comedy has a broad appeal. The movie seems to be made to play to two entirely different audiences. Those who enjoy watching a "Mama" character slap around the "man- boy" and those that like Tom Hanks dressed as Colonel Sanders saying "Roh,koko". Mixing high brow and low brow is nothing new to the Coen Bros. but it's never been as obvious as it is here. I feel compelled to mention that the movie looks fabulous. Shots of the police station look so flat they seem almost 2 dimensional. The colors are muted but almost cartoon-like. And the hiphop and gospel soundtrack really further establishes the duality of the movie. I'm sorry if I have not helped you decide whether to see it or not. I will do so now: Watch it. If you don't have a stick up your arse, you'll find enough SOMETHING here to make you smile. Because this is a movie that looks and feels like a FILM.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Aaron Sticht</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Disappointing</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Having seen many of the Coen brothers films and seen practically everything that Tom Hanks has ever done, I was excited with anticipation in seeing the collaboration of this team. I was left disappointed and felt roped in to seeing a film that was OK at best. The movie starts out in a very disjointed way as the main characters are introduced to the audience with harsh edits between scenes. The audience is not given any explanation as to what they are seeing or why they are seeing it. It is only about 25 minutes into the film that Tom Hanks character (The Professor Dorr) actually introduces the members of his band (of criminals). The whole premise of this film is that a group of smalltime criminals sets up operations in an elderly black womans house to tunnel next door into the vaults of a small riverboat casino. The elderly womans character is the only character in the film which the audience connects with and can actually relate to. For the most part all of the criminals characters are drawn up very two dimensional including the character played by Tom Hanks. I have read some reviews stating that Tom looks like he had more fun doing this film than he has had since making Forrest Gump back in the mid nineties. Nevertheless, the Coen brothers fascination with morbid humor does show occasionally throughout the film as well as their decision to use period type folk music to enhance their soundtrack is typical of such films as Raising Arizona and O' brother where Art Thou? The comedic moments in this film contrary to the trailers I have seen are few and far between and I was left with a feeling of wanting more to make this film complete. Having never seen the original movie that this version is based on I can only suggest to the Coen brothers that they start writing their own material again as they seem to have a much better time at it when they come up with everything from scratch. I also felt that there was an excessive amount of profanity in this film that I cannot remember from other films the Coen brothers have made. Ultimately a disappointing film, lucky it was on discount night at the theater or I would feel more cheated than I already do. I won't even consider purchasing the DVD when it comes out!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>photogenic</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Comedic Southern Gothic Masterpiece</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Wow, I can't believe the negative comments this film is getting. My wife and I saw The Ladykillers this afternoon and we both loved it. First, forget that this is a caper movie. The casino heist only serves to offer a minimal plot over which to drape a great cast of characters, impeccable art direction and cinematography, dark, broad humor, and the final trademark of a Coen Bros. film: virtuoso use of language. The film is neatly bookended by Ms. Munson's visit to the County Sheriff, which at the start of the film immediately introduces her rich character. Soon we're off on the Coen Bros. flights of verbal fancy, which enter earth orbit with the introduction of Tom Hanks' Prof. Dorr character. Follow his words closely, for they require a near-encyclopedic knowledge of academia, classical language and allusions, and Southern idiom. But this is not an intellectual exercise... it's all part of the fun, just like translating Hi's faux regionalisms in Raising Arizona.I guess I could go totally P.C. and decry the broadness of Ms. Munson's black southern churchgoer, the "stereotypical" urban street lowlife played by Marlon Wayans, or the fun had at the expense of Irrital Bowel Syndrom sufferers. This is a broad, dark comedy, people. Rarely have I heard the the word m*th*rf*ck*r used with such panache and brio. And the look of distress upon Garth Pancake's face as he suffers an IBS attack is exquisite.And, oh, the trademark Coen visual setpieces. The opening and closing shots of the County Jail, already mentioned. The bridge spanning the river and the plunge of objects to the trash barges below. The glory that is Ms. Munson's parlor and the portrait of her deceased husband that dominates it.So watch the *whole* movie, people... throw out your preconceptions of what a Tom Hanks role should be, what a caper movie should be, or even that this is a remake of a highly regarded heist flick. Revel in the art direction and set decoration, the ear for language, the broadly drawn characters, and the wonderful photography. This is a Coen Bros. film and we are blessed to have another.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>THE LADYKILLERS (2004) ***1/2 Tom Hanks, Irma P. Hall, Marlon Wayans, JK Simmons, Tzi Ma, Ryan Hurst, Diane Delano, George Wallace, Stephen Root. The Coen Brothers nutty and very broad interpretation of the cult classic Alec Guinness Brit comedy about a gang of burglars who manipulate a sweet old lady (Hall, a fine straight woman foil) by posing as musicians to gain entry to her root cellar which leads to their target: a casino's bank vault worth millions. Hanks scores his goofiest role to date (all Van Dyke/odd teeth and stifling, sniggering guffaws) as the quintet's ringleader, a Southern-fried (think the bastard son of Colonel Sanders!) self-proclaimed professor of classics whose mellifluous pontifications sooth-says the slapstick proceedings at hand with cool-head and silver tongued deviltry. Funny and inventive with a wonderful production design by Dennis Gassner, lovely cinematography by Roger Deakins and a comic ensemble of truly motley proportions.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-03-29</date>
		<username>mr_movie_2004</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Only the Coen brothers</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In what I would call their third "all out" comedy following Raising Arizona and The Big Lebowski, the Coens have once again concocted a screwball comedy with a style all their own, and once again they have succeeded in creating a genuinely funny film.The premise here is, by definition, unoriginal. Not only is it a remake, but the same basic story (minus the old woman) has been told countless times. The reality is, this doesn't really matter. The Ladykillers is unlike any other heist film ever made, including the original. The Coens have created a truly bizarre and hilarious cast of characters, with Hanks masterfully playing the incredibly anachronistic ringleader, a man whose vocabulary is unmatched and who is endlessly sly and cunniving. Irma P. Hall does excellent work as Hanks' adversary, the old woman whose room he is renting. The clash of the characters' personalities and the onscreen chemistry between Hanks and Hall is perfect.The rest of the crew consists of a ragtag bunch of stereotypes who have absolutely nothing in common besides their goal of robbing the casino vault. All of these characters are well cast, and the constant bickering between J.K. Simmons' Garth Pancake and Marlon Wayans' Gawain McSam is the source of amusement throughout.In the end, this is a movie where the storyline doesn't really matter; each point in the plot serves more as an excuse for amusing character vignettes. It works because the Coens have once again come through with a script filled with dialogue that switches between witty and excessively verbose to crude and crass, yet remains funny all the way through. But this is a film that will either be loved or hated. The Coens brand of humor is not for everyone, and I imagine that a large number of the older moviegoers who went to see it just because Tom Hanks was starring will be offended by a good deal of the language, and I'm sure others will be offended by the very intentional stereotypes that are portrayed throughout the movie, but anyone who is a fan of the humor in Raising Arizona and/or The Big Lebowski will enjoy this off the wall Coen brothers creation.</fullReview>
	</review>
